U.S. News -- Capital Account: No Loser as Currencies Gyrate
U.S. News -- Capital Account: No Loser as Currencies Gyrate
By Greg Ip
945 words
12 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It's not a currency war if everyone wins.
The euro has fallen to near a 12-year low against the dollar this week since the European Central Bank
began its long-awaited bond-buying program. The cheap euro is great for Europe: It bolsters exports and thus
growth, and raises the price of imports, thereby warding off deflation.
Feelings are more mixed in the U.S. The greenback's strength against the euro and a host of other currencies
will trim U.S. growth, squeeze exports and make it harder for the Federal Reserve to get inflation back up to
its 2% target.
This is the sort of trade-off that arouses talk of currency wars -- one country helping itself by hurting others, a
pattern reminiscent of "beggar-thy-neighbor" devaluations in the 1930s.
But this is a misreading of the 1930s, and of the present.
In both the 1930s and now, when one country pursues a stimulative monetary policy that depresses its
currency, it often prods others to do the same -- and the result is a global monetary boost that helps
everyone.
At the start of the 1930s, most currencies' values were fixed to gold, which meant they were also fixed to
each other. When Britain abandoned gold in 1931, several trading partners and former colonies followed suit.
Their exports gained an advantage against those that stayed on gold, including France and the U.S.
This, however, wasn't the principal benefit of devaluation. To keep its currency pegged to gold often forced a
central bank to maintain painfully high interest rates. Once off gold, a central bank could lower interest
rates and print money. Barry Eichengreen of the University of California, Berkeley says the resulting
monetary boost helped reverse deflation as well as boost asset prices and investment. This, in turn,
cushioned the fall in imports, offsetting some of the harm that devaluation inflicted on others.
The right response of other countries, Mr. Eichengreen writes, was to meet "monetary expansion with
monetary expansion and currency depreciation with currency depreciation." Eventually, they did respond: The
U.S. devalued in 1933, and France in 1936.
In the modern era, a country trying to manage its exchange rates typically buys or sells its currency in the
market, or uses capital controls or other regulations to restrict its use. Such practices are still common among
emerging markets, notably China.
But that is not going on now: Neither the U.S. nor ECB has intervened to push down its own currencies since
before 2007. Japan hasn't since 2011. (Some smaller countries, notably Switzerland, South Korea and
Denmark, have regularly intervened.) Capital markets in all three are wide open.
Rather, the nations have influenced exchange rates indirectly. By lowering interest rates, a country can
encourage spending and investment and also make its currency less appealing, which is good for trade. With
short-term interest rates close to zero, central banks have instead bought bonds with newly printed dollars,
yen and euros.
This so-called quantitative easing, or QE, has been the real source of tension. When the Fed began its
second round of QE in 2010, it sent the dollar down sharply. Accusations of a global "currency war" filled the
air.
Yet focusing solely on the dollar's fall gives a blinkered view. As the Fed bought bonds, their yields fell, and
so investors rushed to other markets in search of better returns. While that pushed those countries'
currencies up, it also pushed their interest rates down and stock prices higher. This wasn't always welcome,

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
as some of those countries were combating inflation and asset bubbles. Nonetheless, the International
Monetary Fund concluded the net effect of these impacts on the rest of the world was positive.
The effects didn't stop there. The falling dollar prodded other countries to follow the Fed's lead. It was one of
the reasons the Bank of Japan expanded its QE in 2013, after Shinzo Abe became prime minister and
appointed a new bank governor.
American and Japanese QE buoyed the euro and nudged down European inflation, which set the stage for
the ECB to implement a negative short-term interest rate in mid-2014, and then begin its own QE. Those
moves have had potent spillovers. Denmark, Sweden and Switzerland have all introduced negative interest
rates to counter the upward pressure on their currency caused by the ECB's actions.
The spillovers today from the ECB's QE may be less pronounced than those from the U.S. a few years
earlier.
Many of the benefits from a lower euro are flowing to Germany, the eurozone member that least needs them,
and the downward pressure on global interest rates is being counteracted by expectations the Fed will soon
raise interest rates. If China, whose currency has risen thanks to its peg to the dollar, moves to devalue, that
likely will inflame relations with its trading partners.
But if China stays put, then the U.S. is in no position to complain. The weakness of the yen and euro are
simply the mirror image of the same stimulus it enacted more than four years ago.
That stimulus has paid dividends in the form of much stronger job growth which, along with lower oil prices,
means the American economy should easily absorb the hit of a stronger dollar. Because the dollar's climb is
putting further downward pressure on inflation, it may encourage the Fed to keep interest rates at zero a
little longer, which would be a sign the ECB's monetary stimulus has crossed the Atlantic.

Worries About Greece Give Lift to Gold Price
Worries About Greece Give Lift to Gold Price
By Tatyana Shumsky
384 words
10 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold prices recovered from a three-month low Monday as traders refocused on concerns
surrounding Greece's financial problems.
The most actively traded contract, for April delivery, rose $2.20, or 0.2%, to settle at $1,166.50 a troy ounce
on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Gold prices rebounded from their steepest drop in more than a year as investors shifted sights from the U.S.
to Greece. On Friday, prices tumbled 2.7% to $1,164.30 an ounce after strong U.S. jobs data bolstered the
case for the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates this year. But by Monday, investors focused on the risk
that Greece will hold a referendum on whether to accept its creditors' financial-aid terms if the government
decides they are acceptable.
"The market was wrangling with the fact that we could be a step closer to a higher interest-rate environment,
and now the Greek economic crisis is once again on the forefront of industrious minds," said Dave Meger,
director of metal trading with High Ridge Futures in Chicago.
The long-running fear for many traders is that Greece would leave the euro bloc to default on its massive debt
load and destabilize the common currency. Gold has benefited from these fears as the precious metal is
seen as likely to keep its value better than other assets during periods of financial and economic uncertainty.
Meanwhile, the European Central Bank began its bond-buying program on Monday, a move that is likely to
spur demand for gold as a store of value.
The stimulus effort is likely to weaken the euro against the dollar. But while dollar-denominated gold prices
are likely to fall in response, gold priced in other currencies, such as the euro, is likely to rise, making it a
lucrative investment for foreign buyers, Mr. Meger said.
Elsewhere, platinum prices extended their slump to a fresh 5 1/2-year low. Platinum for April delivery fell
$10.20, or 0.9%, to close at $1,148.60 a troy ounce on the Nymex.
Platinum prices have been under pressure in recent months as supply from South Africa recovers from the
industry's longest strike.

Stocks Gain After Selloff Attracts Buyers --- Shares sensitive to higher interest rates, such as utilities and REITs, post rebounds
Monday's Markets
Stocks Gain After Selloff Attracts Buyers --- Shares sensitive to higher interest rates, such as utilities
and REITs, post rebounds
By Dan Strumpf
719 words
10 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
General Motors Co. shares rose 2.2% to $37.66 on Monday. A Monday's Markets article Tuesday about the
company incorrectly said the stock rose 3.1%.
(WSJ March 14, 2015)
(END)
U.S. stocks advanced, after a selloff that followed strong employment news on Friday attracted buyers back
into the market.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 138.94 points, or 0.8%, to 17995.72, while the S&P 500 index rose
8.17, or 0.4%, to 2079.43. The Nasdaq Composite Index climbed 15.07, or 0.3%, to 4942.44.
Overseas early Tuesday, Japan's Nikkei was up 0.5% on a lower yen, while Hong Kong's Hang Seng index
was down 02.%. Benchmarks in South Korea, Malaysia and Taiwan were up.
Friday's broad declines in the U.S. occurred after a strong jobs report cemented expectations for a Federal
Reserve interest-rate increase later this year.
"It's another case of people coming in and buying the dip," said Viren Chandrasoma, managing director of
equity trading at Credit Suisse. "At the end of the day, buying the dip has worked for the last five years since
the crisis."
With Monday's rebound, the Dow is 1.6% below its most recent high set on March 2, and the S&P is off 1.8%
from its record set the same day.
U.S. payrolls rose by a seasonally adjusted 295,000 in February, the Labor Department said Friday, while the
unemployment rate fell to 5.5%, the lowest since May 2008 and within the Fed's target zone for rate
increases.
Many investors believe the Fed will raise interest rates during the second half of 2015. However, with
inflation low, the Fed is expected to move slowly.
"It just feels like people are starting to shrug off the Fed," said Tom Carter, managing director and trader at
JonesTrading. "The economy is strong. Unemployment is going down. All the numbers have come in OK, and
we're going higher."
Stocks sensitive to higher interest rates, such as utilities and real-estate investment trusts, rose after falling
sharply Friday. The iShares U.S. Real Estate Exchange-Traded Fund gained 0.9%. Utilities shares within the
S&P 500 rose 0.1%.
Many investors say stocks can continue to rise this year, though sluggish economic growth overseas and lofty
valuations in the U.S. have some expecting more muted gains than in years past.
The S&P 500 index now trades at 17.3 times the last 12 months' worth of earnings, according to FactSet.
That compares with an average P/E ratio of 15.8 over the past 10 years.
"Valuations in the U.S. are pretty high," said Dan Morris, global investment strategist at TIAA-CREF, which
manages $611 billion in assets. "Our expectations for the market over the next couple of months aren't
optimistic."

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Morris said stocks could post losses in the months ahead, though he is bullish on certain sectors,
including technology stocks.
Among individual stocks, Alcoa said it would acquire RTI International Metals in a deal valued at $1.5 billion.
Alcoa shares fell 78 cents, or 5.4%, to $13.70 while those of RTI soared 10.72, or 39%, to 38.
General Motors gained 1.12, or 3.1%, to 37.66, after the company said it would begin buying back $5 billion
of stock in a deal that avoids a proxy fight with investor Harry J. Wilson.
European stocks were mixed. France's CAC-40 closed with a loss of 0.5%, while Germany's DAX rose 0.3%
to a record.
In other markets, gold futures rose 0.2% to $1,166.40 an ounce. Crude-oil futures gained 0.8% to $50 a
barrel.
In the bond market, U.S. Treasury prices rose along with their peers in Europe, as the European Central
Bank launched its bond-buying program. Rock-bottom yields in Europe lured buyers to the U.S., where yields
are relatively higher. On Monday, a buyer of U.S. government bonds obtained the highest yield premium in
more than two decades against German bonds.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- Monthly Monitor: U.S. Stock Funds Rose 5.6% During the February Rally, And Even Overseas...
Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- Monthly Monitor: U.S. Stock Funds Rose 5.6%
During the February Rally, And Even Overseas Joined In
By William Power
417 words
9 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
R2
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It was a "We Are the World" market for investors in February, as U.S. stocks rallied and even struggling
foreign-stock mutual funds joined in with comparable gains.
Stock-market skeptics are bracing for the inevitable pullback in the market after the long bull run. But for now,
U.S.-stock funds have recovered easily from January's declines. Investors are content with stronger U.S.
economic indicators and the Federal Reserve's signals of a reasonable approach to interest-rate increases
this year. (And even if the stock-market bear makes an appearance, writes the Journal's veteran stock-market
reporter E.S. Browning on today's cover, long-term investors shouldn't fear.)
The average diversified U.S.-stock mutual fund rose 5.6% in February, making the year-to-date gain 2.8%.
Foreign-stock funds had been struggling on average, despite their popularity, but even that category had a
good February, up 5.5% and now up 5.8% for the year so far.
"A great deal of the optimism is coming from Europe," says Mark Baribeau, head of global equity at Jennison
Associates, and co-manager of Prudential Jennison Global Opportunities Fund. The prospect of European
Central Bank support through bond buying (quantitative easing) "has drawn a very strong moat around
Europe in terms of at least providing some positive outlook for sustainable growth."
Still, it isn't a great economic environment around the world, he cautions. So there is no comfort in merely
having overseas exposure in a portfolio these days. "It's more important to invest in funds that have some
sort of unique strategy."
Meanwhile, gold-focused funds were drubbed, but that is their up-and-down nature as a portfolio protector.
After gaining 13% in January, they fell 3.4% in February (and on Friday, gold prices slid nearly 3%).
Bond funds fell in the month, after a strong January. The most widely held variety, funds that focus on
intermediate-term, investment-grade debt, had an average negative return of 0.7%, trimming the year-to-date
gain to 1.3%. Bond investors are holding on to their hats that any rate increases aren't too jarring.
---
Mr. Power is a Wall Street Journal news editor in South Brunswick, N.J. Email him at
william.power@wsj.com.

Denmark Intervenes in a Big Way --- Central bank purchases a record amount of foreign currency to protect the krone's peg
Currency Trading
Denmark Intervenes in a Big Way --- Central bank purchases a record amount of foreign currency to
protect the krone's peg
By Brian Blackstone and Emese Bartha
635 words
4 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Denmark's central bank bought a record amount of foreign currency last month in a bid to protect the krone's
peg to the euro, underscoring the fallout from the European Central Bank's efforts to boost economic growth.
The Danmarks Nationalbank's net purchases of foreign exchange amounted to 168.7 billion Danish kroner
($25.32 billion) in February, equal to about 9% of the Nordic country's gross domestic product.
The scale of the intervention was a record for Denmark's central bank, which also made significant net
purchases of foreign currency the previous month.
"The [central bank] has never bought or sold foreign exchange to this extent before, which shows that the
krone has been under very significant appreciation pressure in recent months," said Citigroup economist Tina
Mortensen in a note.
The central bank said the bulk of interventions took place during days around its interest-rate reduction on
Feb. 5. The central bank said its foreign-exchange reserves increased by 172.9 billion kroner in February, to
737.1 billion kroner. Foreign assets, a subset of foreign-exchange reserves that excludes categories including
gold, surged to 703.58 billion kroner at Feb. 28, from 442.77 billion a year earlier.
Even if the intervention tapered off in February, "it is too early to say that the battle is over," Nomura analyst
Jens Nordvig said in a research note. "The Danish central bank may need to consider extreme measures to
reinforce the peg further," such as temporary capital controls or transaction taxes, he wrote. The central
bank doesn't give a specific breakdown of the currencies it buys or sells, but Mr. Nordvig said the purchases
were almost all for euros.
A representative for Denmark's central bank told The Wall Street Journal last month that the bank is
standing firm on the peg. "The peg is a cornerstone of Danish economic policy and has been so since 1982,
and there is a broad commitment throughout Denmark for the fixed exchange rate," he said.
The central bank aims to keep the euro's exchange rate within 2.25% above or below 7.46038 kroner to
keep a lid on inflation and provide stability for domestic businesses that look to the eurozone as an important
export market. The peg has existed in close to its current form for more than three decades, first relative to
the German currency and later against the euro.
But the peg has come under pressure as investors poured funds into kroner recently in search of better
returns than they can get in the eurozone. The ECB's planned bond-buying program has pushed eurozone
bond yields down and led to a decline in the value of the euro. Bond yields move inversely to prices.
Denmark has cut its key policy rates four times this year, with the certificate of deposit rate most recently
reduced by 0.25 percentage point to minus-0.75%. When monetary authorities set a negative interest rate on
bank deposits, financial institutions, in effect, pay a fee to park their excess funds with the central bank.
In other measures aimed at deflecting foreign inflows, the central bank has suspended government-bond
issuance and rejected bids at some recent treasury auctions.
The ECB's decision in January to purchase more than 1 trillion euros ($1.12 trillion) in public and private
bonds has put significant upward pressure on haven currencies outside the eurozone, including the krone
and the Swiss franc.
In January, the Swiss National Bank abandoned the ceiling it had set on the franc's value against the euro, as
defending that cap had become too costly.

India's Failure to Cut Import Duty on Gold Hurts Prices
Commodities Report
India's Failure to Cut Import Duty on Gold Hurts Prices
By Tatyana Shumsky and Biman Mukherji
553 words
3 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
India's decision to maintain an import duty on gold surprised investors, dealing a fresh blow to a metal that
has been hit hard this year by a strengthening U.S. dollar and a series of policy shifts around the globe.
Gold for April delivery, the most actively traded contract, sank $4.90, or 0.4%, to $1,208.20 a troy ounce on
the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Investors had been hoping India would reduce the duty, likely boosting demand for gold from the world's
largest consumer. The decision adds to broad crosscurrents for gold, which tends to appreciate at times of
economic uncertainty but does less well in more placid periods because it offers buyers no periodic
payments, or yield.
"You can add this to the long list of disappointments for gold," said Vedant Mimani, who helps manage $80
million for ACGO Funds in Miami. Mr. Mimani has been neutral on gold this year, believing U.S. monetary
policy will play a central role in determining price direction in the market. "Gold keeps trying to hang its hat
on some sort of change or something positive around the corner that doesn't seem to materialize," he said.
Gold prices have slid 7.1% from a five-month high of $1,300.70 an ounce on Jan. 22, the day the European
Central Bank announced a 60 billion-a-month ($67.2 billion-a-month) plan to buy bonds to bolster European
growth.
Gold's downdraft comes as central banks outside the U.S. race to trim domestic interest rates in a bid to
bolster flagging growth. While efforts to ease monetary policy typically would be bullish for gold prices, the
expectation that the U.S. Federal Reserve will raise interest rates is trumping those moves, with gold bugs
worrying that higher yields in the U.S. will dim gold's allure. At the same time, Europe's easy-money policies
may help the region skirt a damaging period of deflation, an economically enervating cycle of falling wages
and prices and slowing business and consumer spending, and remove the need for investors there to seek
the safety of gold.
"Gold has been moving in exactly the opposite direction to the dollar and acting as a currency hedge over the
last few weeks," said Paul Nolte, who helps manage nearly $100 million at Kingsview Asset Management in
Chicago.
The soaring dollar makes gold more expensive for buyers in other countries, including India, as they must
exchange more of their home currency for dollars to buy the metal on the global market. Many investors also
load up on gold in periods of financial instability, and while some worries linger, many are switching back to
riskier assets such as bonds and stocks as confidence improves in the U.S. economy.
India's gold imports had slowed in the weeks leading up to the budget announcement, as some wholesalers
delayed purchases in anticipation of a tax cut. While imports are expected to rise in the near term now that
the uncertainty is removed, some traders said Indian gold demand is likely to be lower over this year as the
import tax tempers investors' interest.

Big Issues in Personal Finance (A Special Report) --- Do Cryptocurrencies Such as Bitcoin Have a Future?
Big Issues in Personal Finance (A Special Report) --- Do Cryptocurrencies Such as Bitcoin Have a
Future?
1,686 words
2 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Despite the mystery, the whiff of scandal, and general public unfamiliarity with the concept, somebody out
there is buying, and selling, not just bitcoin but dozens of other cryptocurrencies as well. The total market
capitalization for these unregulated electronic forms of payment was roughly $4.04 billion as of mid-February,
according to coinmarketcap.com, a website that tracks trading in alternative currencies. More than 500
altcoins, as they are also known, were represented on the site recently.
Growing global demand for low-cost, swift transactions -- by cutting out banks -- is one of the main forces
driving interest in alternative currencies in recent years. Another, of course, is pure speculation.
Perhaps the most common criticism of cryptocurrencies is that, unlike traditional currencies, they have no
basic underlying value. Supporters will counter that, like a lot of investments, a cryptocurrency is worth
whatever the market says it is.
But that's an argument that can cut both ways, especially in bitcoin's case: Since reaching roughly $1,147 in
December 2013, the value of a bitcoin, the leading cryptocurrency by market cap, has plunged about 80%.
Time will tell which alternative currencies, if any, will grow in acceptance and value.
Are we at the start of an era in which new monetary systems will be adopted?
Or should we keep our hands on our wallets?

Yes: Don't Judge Bitcoin by Its Early, Inevitable Problems
----
By Campbell R. Harvey
Successful innovations solve important problems. Here are some of the problems that bitcoins solve.
-- Bitcoins allow online transactions without worrying that personal data (such as credit-card or bank-account
details) will be compromised.
-- Anyone with a cellphone or Internet connection can execute seamless transactions, even in amounts less
than a penny. This is important for many who lack bank access, especially in the developing world.
-- International remittances carry trivial fees compared with the 10% charged by current firms in this space.
-- Transaction fees for merchants are minimal, compared with credit-card fees, which is good for retailers with
narrow margins.
-- And there is no inflation risk. The rate at which bitcoins are created is slow, and the total of coins that can
be created is limited, according to the mathematical dictates of the system. Bitcoin provides an alternative
way to transact for countries experiencing economic or political stress.
True, bitcoin isn't backed by any central authority. But that doesn't matter. Bitcoin exists because users
assign value to it. To say it violates the rules of finance because it lacks a central issuer is problematic on
many levels. Governments don't "guarantee" stability of their currencies -- look at the ruble and Swiss franc.
Similarly, the fair price of a bitcoin as measured by the discounted value of future cash flows may be zero.
But the same is true of fiat currencies, including the euro and U.S. dollar. No commodity underpins the value
of a euro or dollar. You tend to lose money when you hold cash. This doesn't deter people from holding cash.

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But forget about exchange rates for a moment. The most exciting thing about bitcoin is the technology behind
it, the block chain, an online, transparent record of every transaction on every bitcoin. It is a giant electronic
financial ledger that is used to authenticate each transaction and, in the process, produce more bitcoins.
The block chain's potential reaches far beyond bitcoin the currency. It is a way to both verify ownership and to
set up contracts. Imagine getting into your car. Through your mobile device, the car locates your identity code
and proof of purchase in the block chain. This allows the car to start. But say you have missed three
payments on your car loan. Additional terms in the block chain make sure the car will only start for the person
or institution that lent you the money.
Making contracts part of a block chain would be easy by introducing "if, then" statements to the chain in
computer code.
Almost any financial instrument, including stocks, bonds or options, could be represented and made verifiable
in such a format.
Just as block-chain technology has created a currency that operates without need of a bank, it could help
users get loans without a bank, or make investments without a broker or exchange.
Yes, the value of a bitcoin has fallen sharply over the past year. However, it is a serious mistake to judge
bitcoin's future based on the movement in the exchange rate. A better indicator is the activity of highly
respected venture capitalists who are pouring money into hundreds of bitcoin- and block-chain related
ventures.
It is true that bitcoin is currently too volatile to be a long-term store of value. But much of this is due to
illiquidity, which is not unexpected when the technology is so new. A regulated, insured, U.S.-based bitcoin
exchange opened in January that is backed by the New York Stock Exchange, which should add liquidity and
reduce volatility.
There are risks, as the Mt. Gox debacle showed, when hundreds of millions of dollars in bitcoins vanished
from the failed exchange. But with a nascent technology, many new firms enter the industry -- some good and
some bad. As bitcoin develops, it is likely the number of firms following best practices that safeguard people's
money will grow. The support of experienced venture capitalists also increases the chance that high-quality
firms will arrive.
Finally, many associate bitcoin with illegal transactions. The real target for illegal transactions should be cash.
Cash is anonymous and private. There is no ledger detailing every transaction with cash. Indeed, it is
ridiculous that of all the cash in U.S. dollars circulating, 78% is represented by $100 bills. How many $100
bills are in your wallet?
---
Dr. Harvey is a professor of finance at Duke University in Durham, N.C. He can be reached at
reports@wsj.com.
---
No: As a Currency, Bitcoin Violates All The Rules of Finance
----
By Eric Tymoigne
Bitcoins are an odd sort of commodity. They are not financial instruments. The value fluctuates widely, in line
with changing views regarding the overall usefulness of the bitcoin payment system and the speculative
manias surrounding such views. There is no financial logic behind bitcoins' face value.
In other words, if you like to gamble, this is a perfect asset. If you are looking for an alternative monetary
instrument, look elsewhere.
The bitcoin system has two components: the means of payment themselves, and an online ledger, called the
block chain, which is a record of all bitcoins that have been created and who holds them. The ledger is the
main innovation. It provides an open, decentralized, fast, cheap and supposedly secure means of completing
transactions.
But as an alleged alternative currency, bitcoin is unacceptable. Its volatility and lack of liquidity pose risks far
beyond most traditional currencies.

--- Page 17 ---
Page 17 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To understand why, take a quick look at how real money works. Monetary instruments are securities. As
such, they have a term to maturity (instantaneous) and an issuer -- often a central bank or private banks --
that promises to pay the bearer the full face value. Gold coins are a collateralized form of such security.
Paper, cheap metal, and electronic entries are the forms such securities take today. The characteristics of
these securities allow them to circulate at a stable nominal value (par) in the right financial infrastructure and
as long as the creditworthiness of the issuer is strong. This provides a reliable means to complete
transactions and, more important, service debts.
Bitcoins, meanwhile, violate all of the rules of finance. There is no central issuer guaranteeing payment at
face value to the bearer; in fact, there is no underlying face value, and subsequently no imputed value at
maturity, which means they are completely impractical for use in servicing of debt. The fair price of bitcoins as
measured by the discounted value of future cash flows is zero.
Bitcoins pose a huge liquidity risk. Ultimately, anyone with bitcoins has to convert them into a national unit of
account -- dollars, say, or euros -- to pay taxes or personal debts and to make other transactions. Their
extreme volatility makes them a bad bet if one plans to buy a house in a few years, is saving for college, or
has regular payments on, say, a mortgage or car. If bitcoins were a large asset in a portfolio, the investor's
solvency would be at risk. This certainly would be the case if bitcoins were promoted for poorer individuals
who don't have access to banking today.
For an economy to work well, money needs to be created (for example, through bank credits or government
spending) and withdrawn (through debt servicing and tax payments) following economic logic. We have all
seen recently, in the global financial collapse of 2008-09, how irresponsible behavior on the part of big banks
with regard to their lending and debt-servicing practices can set off widespread financial panic followed by
years of economic stagnation.
The mechanics of creating and withdrawing money need to operate not only with sound economic logic. They
also should be simple, to accommodate quickly the needs of a flexible economy. Today, money is created
and destroyed in seconds through digital entries.
Bitcoins, by contrast, are created using a purely mathematical logic that lacks financial or economic
underpinnings (currently 25 new bitcoins every 10 minutes); and they can't be retired as needed to maintain
their scarcity. Given the lack of economic logic behind the net injection of bitcoins, there is increased risk of
financial and price instability.
The block chain is useful as an authentication tool and is the main innovation. But it's too soon to tell whether
it can have other applications. For now, unfortunately, it's a potential step forward accompanied by an actual
step backward.
---
Mr. Tymoigne is an assistant professor of economics at Lewis & Clark College in Portland, Ore. He can be
reached at reports@wsj.com.

U.S. News: Yellen Meets With Conservative Group
By Pedro Nicolaci da Costa
428 words
28 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A group of conservative activists worried the Federal Reserve has left interest rates too low for too long
voiced their concerns directly to Janet Yellen, the central bank's chairwoman, during a meeting Friday.
Ms. Yellen met for about an hour in the Fed's policy meeting room with a group of 21 right-leaning
economists, activists and businesspeople, said Steven Lonegan, director of monetary policy at American
Principles in Action, a conservative think tank in Washington that spearheaded the meeting.
Fed governor Lael Brainard also was present, Mr. Lonegan said.
The Fed declined to comment.
The group's message: The Fed has kept its benchmark short-term interest rate near zero for so long -- since
December 2008 -- that the policy is now having the unintended effect of damping employment rather than
boosting it. The group said low rates encourage companies to invest in equipment and structures rather than
hire more workers.
Marc Miles, economist at Global Economic Solutions in Boston and a speaker in the meeting with Ms. Yellen,
said in a statement before the meeting that "low interest rates produce distortions or unintended
consequences that exacerbate some of our current economic problems" such as high unemployment.
This worsens the problem of U.S. income inequality, said Mr. Lonegan, who was mayor of Bogota, N.J., for
12 years.
"We wanted to let her know it's not just the left that cares about the middle class and low-income Americans,"
Mr. Lonegan said after the meeting.
According to the American Principles in Action website, Mr. Lonegan is head of a project called Fix the Dollar,
"charged with the responsibility of building national grass-roots support for sound monetary policy and
restoring the gold standard."
Ms. Yellen has previously met with liberal activists at the Fed.
Republican lawmakers accused Ms. Yellen at a House Financial Services Committee hearing Wednesday of
favoring Democrats, citing her official calendar, which shows she held more meetings over the past year with
executive branch officials than with members of Congress, and met with more Democratic lawmakers than
Republicans.
Rep. Scott Garrett, a New Jersey Republican, told Ms. Yellen during the hearing that her calendar, as well as
her meeting with "liberal advocacy groups" and a speech on inequality, were among the factors that painted
for him a picture of partisan bias.
She responded it was a "complete mischaracterization" to portray her schedule and activities as partisan. She
mentioned then that she planned to meet Friday with the conservative group.

Dow Jumps 5.6% for the Month
Friday's Markets
Dow Jumps 5.6% for the Month
By Corrie Driebusch
802 words
28 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks closed out a slow week with a decline, but still ended February with their biggest monthly
percentage gains in more than two years.
For the month, the Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped 5.6%, its best month since January 2013, while the
S&P 500 advanced 5.5%, for its best month since October 2011.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 7.1% in February, positioning it within striking distance of 5000, a level
last touched nearly 15 years ago.
The strong month follows a weak January, when U.S. stocks turned in their biggest monthly losses in a year,
with the Dow losing 3.7% and the S&P 500 falling 3.1%.
"There's more applauding going on for U.S. stocks, and there's more excitement building up around the
market," said Tony Scherrer, director of research at Smead Capital Management, which manages about $1.3
billion.
This excitement can be seen playing out particularly in the Nasdaq index, Mr. Scherrer said, though he has
some concerns. "Some of the young tech, the conceptual tech kind of stocks, we think they're getting
rewarded with too high of multiples and that there's too much excitement around them," he said.
He said his firm prefers some "old tech" names based on lower price/earnings valuations and strong cash
flows.
Despite the big gains in February, trading volumes in recent sessions have been muted and the broad market
has held to a narrow range.
On Friday, the Dow fell 81.72 points, or 0.4%, to 18132.70. The S&P 500 slipped 6.24 points, or 0.3%, to
2104.50 and the Nasdaq Composite declined 24.36 points, or 0.5%, to 4963.53.
The soft tone to stocks on Friday came after the Commerce Department reported U.S. gross domestic
product expanded at a 2.2% annual rate in the fourth quarter, down from an initial estimate of 2.6%.
Separately, the Chicago Business Barometer fell to its lowest point since July 2009, declining to 45.8 in
February from January's 59.4. The decline indicates business activity is contracting.
"The data over the last two months has been more mixed on a macroeconomic front, but as stock investors
you're not thriving off of GDP numbers," said Stephen Freedman, head of cross-asset strategy at UBS
Wealth Management Americas, which manages roughly $1 trillion. "Earnings ultimately are what matters."
With 485 companies in the S&P 500 reporting their results, firms in the index are on track to post a 3.7%
earnings-growth rate for the fourth quarter of 2014 from a year earlier, according to FactSet. That topped
expectations for a 1.7% rise in profits.
In addition to earnings, stocks have gotten a lift from a stabilization in oil prices and an easing of economic
and political concerns about Europe, particularly worries about negotiations between Greece and its creditors.
Investors also have found good news in the outlook for Federal Reserve policy, where the central bank is
expected to stick to a slow path should it start to raise interest rates sometime this year.
For the month, the best-performing sector in the S&P 500 was consumer discretionary, with shares of those
companies in the index rising 8.5%. Investors have been putting money in the group in part because they
view the segment as benefiting from lower oil prices.

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We're a believer that cheaper gas puts more money into peoples' pockets, and people have a tendency to
spend what's in their pockets," said Tom Stringfellow, chief investment officer of Frost Investment Advisors,
which manages about $10 billion. He said that consumer discretionary is currently one of the sectors he likes
the most.
Technology stocks were the second-biggest gainers for the month, rising 5.5%. Utilities stocks, known as
bond proxies because they pay out big dividends, have tumbled the most in February, falling 7%.
In other markets, gold futures rose 0.2% on Friday to $1,212.60 an ounce. Action was muted in the Treasury
market. The yield on the 10-year note slipped to 2.002%, from 2.016% on Thursday. Yields rise as prices fall.
In corporate news, J.C. Penney shares tumbled 62 cents, or 6.8%, to $8.50 after the retailer reported a
surprise loss for the fourth quarter.
Weight Watchers International swung to a loss in the fourth quarter as the company continued to lose
members. The weight-loss company's shares fell 6.23, or 35%, to 11.33.
Ross Stores shares rose 6.76, or 6.8%, to 105.81 after the retailer reported sales and earnings well above its
expectations.

fourth-quarter corporate earnings, improved U.S. economic data and expectations that the Federal Reserve

going to redeploy it."

Gas Boom Gone Bust Leaves Orphaned Wells --- Wyoming cleans up after a would-be mogul and his Gazmo device
Gas Boom Gone Bust Leaves Orphaned Wells --- Wyoming cleans up after a would-be mogul and his
Gazmo device
By Dan Frosch and Russell Gold
2,118 words
26 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GILLETTE, Wyo. -- After a natural-gas boom in the Powder River Basin here petered out several years ago,
few energy companies were interested in the leftover wells pockmarking the prairie. Then Ed Presley came
along.
The burly, bearded speculator acquired roughly 3,000 idle wells, many for a few dollars. With a salesman's
charm, he vowed to revive the wells with a contraption called the Gazmo.
But Mr. Presley's plan never produced any gas. He says he couldn't raise enough money for his company,
High Plains Gas Inc., to follow through. Last year, Wyoming seized most of his wells to ensure they didn't
pollute groundwater or soil, declaring them abandoned.
On one ranch near Gillette last month, several of Mr. Presley's former wells peeked from the snow. Inside the
flimsy sheds covering them, jumbles of rusting pipes protruded from the ground, worn company signs
dangling nearby.
Wyoming is now stuck cleaning up these deserted wells from a bygone boom, and thousands more owned by
Mr. Presley and others, at a cost state regulators estimate will be tens of millions of dollars. State officials say
the responsible parties never paid enough in regulatory fees to reclaim the wells.
Wyoming's troubles with Mr. Presley's wells are a cautionary tale for states amid the energy rush. Drilling
booms historically leave legions of idle wells that become state or federal wards. Yet agencies in some
states, and federal regulators, aren't adequately equipped to clean up so-called orphaned sites at a time
when shale drilling is raising the prospects of still more.
Hydraulic fracturing has, for example, brought new drilling in the Marcellus Shale that lies under states like
Pennsylvania and West Virginia. The potential for more orphaned wells "is certainly a concern of ours," says
David Belcher, assistant enforcement chief for West Virginia's oil-and-gas office. "It could be considered a
liability for the state."
Jay Parrish, Pennsylvania's state geologist from 2001 to 2010, says he is concerned Marcellus drilling could
leave that state with a surge in environmentally hazardous wells without enough funds to clean them up.
"We run the risk of doing what we did with the last two iterations of lumber and coal," he says, "where we
allow the industry to walk away from problems and the state is faced with having to fix it later."
Who pays to plug a well?
Abandoned wells can deteriorate underground over time, a process that can go unnoticed without inspection.
A 2011 study by the Groundwater Protection Council, a nonprofit made up of state water agencies, found
orphaned wells caused about one in five incidences of recorded oil and gas groundwater contamination in
Ohio and Texas.
Plugging a well -- removing equipment and filling holes with cement -- costs $25,000 to $100,000 for
conventional sites, by some state regulators' estimates. Horizontal wells, typical in fracking, will likely cost
more to plug, they say.
Mr. Presley's High Plains wells, which are shallow, will cost about $7,500 each to plug, says Bob King,
Wyoming's orphan-wells project manager.
"It is irresponsible to leave them abandoned without dealing with them," says Wyoming Gov. Matt Mead of his
state's problems with orphaned wells. "We shouldn't have to pay for it."

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To avoid having to pay steep costs, most states and the federal government have policies to lay aside funds
to clean up orphans, primarily by requiring companies to post bond before prospecting. But bonding often
sets aside too little, leaving some agencies struggling to clean up tens of thousands of wells.
There is little nationwide data on orphaned wells or on which states face the greatest funding shortfalls for
plugging. Lucas Davis, an associate economics professor at University of California, Berkeley, says current
bonding levels are "unreasonably low" and should be raised in anticipation of abandoned wells from fracking.
"Given the sheer number of wells that are being drilled by companies, many of which are small and medium
sized, states really need to be worried about situations where no company is around anymore," he says.
"Without increased bonding levels, these cleanups will be financed by the state and federal government."
Many energy-rich states are already saddled with having to plug thousands of abandoned wells from past
booms. Pennsylvania's bonding ranges from $2,500 per conventional well to a $600,000 blanket bond for
multiple unconventional wells. In 2014, the state plugged 48 wells from a list of 8,371 orphans, state records
show.
Mr. Parrish, the former Pennsylvania state geologist, says the "bonding is outrageously small" in the state. A
spokeswoman for Pennsylvania's environmental-protection department says the adequacy of that bonding is
under review.
Louisiana from 2008 through 2013 plugged an average 95 wells annually but added an average of 170 a year
to its orphans list, a 2014 state audit showed. The audit found that, because of antiquated regulations and
exemptions, 75% of the state's wells had no bonding on them.
"Not requiring sufficient financial security amounts may provide an incentive for operators to abandon their
wells since forfeiting the financial security may be more economical than paying plugging costs," the audit
said. A Louisiana Department of Natural Resources spokesman says regulators are working to strengthen
bonding requirements.
Texas, which grappled with orphan wells in the 1990s, has required operators since 2001 to post a $2 bond
for every foot of depth or $250,000 to cover numerous wells. Still, the number of abandoned wells in Texas
has grown 25% during the past two years, state figures show. Texas has roughly 9,300 wells it considers
orphans. It plans to plug about 290 this fiscal year.
A spokeswoman for the Texas Railroad Commission, which oversees drilling, says there aren't plans to
review bonding requirements and that the state's program is working, noting that the number of abandoned
wells has dropped about 44% over roughly a decade.
In 2011, West Virginia established stronger bonding for horizontal drilling used in fracking: $50,000 per well or
a blanket $250,000 per driller, compared with $5,000 to $50,000 for conventional drilling.
But the West Virginia Surface Owners' Rights Organization, a landowners' group, says blanket bonding in
particular is still far too low and that the rush to drill the Marcellus could lead to a wave of abandoned wells.
Mr. Belcher, the West Virginia oil regulator, says conditions of such wells will ultimately vary but "on an
average case, you may have an issue with the funding." He says the state is now calculating the cost of
plugging horizontal wells.
Wells like Mr. Presley's often end up orphaned after passing from large companies to smaller ones without
wherewithal to plug them.
Describing himself as a longtime oil man, Mr. Presley, 68, tells of working drilling jobs in Ohio and West
Virginia before heading for the natural-gas rush sweeping the Powder River Basin.
Shortly after arriving in Sheridan, Wyo., in 2001, he says he met Kit Jennings, a Wyoming-state senator at the
time who had patent rights to a technology that purportedly drew methane from coal seams more effectively.
Christening the technology 'The Gazmo,' Mr. Presley told investors he, Mr. Jennings and another partner had
a way to rejuvenate idle wells that larger companies had left for dead. Mr. Jennings confirms that account,
declining further comment.
In 2004, Mr. Presley filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy shortly after a lending company wired him $550,000 for
what proved to be a nonexistent drilling rig. The lender sued him in federal district court in New Mexico,
where the judge ruled he and several others had committed fraud, awarding the lender $550,000.
Mr. Presley says that he wasn't aware the rig was fictitious at the time and that he knows he must pay the
award at some point.

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He says he was certain the Gazmo would succeed, helping him settle debts. His original plan, he says, was:
"We'll just go out there and take wells over and get them for nothing to put them into production."
With natural-gas prices sinking around 2010, he had little trouble finding operators happy to unload
coal-bed-methane wells in the Powder River Basin that were now idle. The first company he acquired, High
Plains, owned about 1,600 idle wells it obtained that year from Pennaco Energy, Securities and Exchange
Commission filings show. Pennaco is a subsidiary of Marathon Oil Corp., which didn't respond to inquiries.
Mr. Presley says High Plains owed Wyoming at least $10 million in fees for idle wells, compliance fines and
unpaid royalties. When Mr. Presley offered to take on the wells in 2013, High Plains' owners agreed to give
him the company at no cost, he says. A lawyer who represented High Plains at the time declines to comment.
Mr. Presley soon after bought wells from Colorado-based Patriot Energy Resources originally drilled by
Devon Energy Corp., a large Oklahoma City company.
In 2013, Patriot's parent, Luca Technologies Inc., filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy. Luca agreed to sell Patriot
and its approximately 1,350 wells to Mr. Presley for $10 last March, bankruptcy-court records show. Matt
Micheli, then Luca's general counsel, says Mr. Presley's offer made sense, given the company's problems.
"We didn't have any real choice."
When the deal closed, Mr. Presley became an abandoned-well mogul.
He held a pancake breakfast for landowners, vowing to make High Plans wells on their land productive again.
Some attendees who had seen drillers come and go had doubts. "He was a good talker if you believe in fairy
tales," says one, Bill West, a local rancher.
Mr. Presley says that he understood the skepticism but that his interest was different from bigger companies'.
"We are not carpetbaggers. We're locals."
Still, he couldn't raise $6.75 million in additional bonding, which Wyoming's Oil and Gas Conservation
Commission required because the wells had been idle for so long. In November, the commission seized High
Plains' wells.
For Wyoming, Mr. Presley's sites add to a growing list of abandoned wells. Orphans on state and private land
more than doubled last year to nearly 3,900. State officials estimate it could cost from $22 million to more
than $40 million to clean them up. State figures show about $9.7 million in bonding for those wells.
Wyoming's oil-and-gas commission hopes to plug more than 650 abandoned wells in 2015. Last year, the
state allocated an additional $3 million to deal with the problem. Wyoming officials say they expect eventually
to consider raising the energy-production tax on operators and increasing bonding amounts, to help fund
plugging.
"It is important to plug these wells quickly so that there are reduced opportunities for groundwater
contamination," says Tom Drean, the state geologist and a commission member.
The U.S. Bureau of Land Management, meanwhile, faces 651 idle High Plains wells it hasn't taken
possession of yet on federal land in Wyoming. The company owes it over $15 million in bonding, civil fines
and penalties on those wells, some from before Mr. Presley took over, BLM officials say. Mr. Presley doesn't
dispute the fees.
The BLM hopes an operator takes over the wells before it deems them fully abandoned, says Duane
Spencer, who manages its Buffalo, Wyo., field office. The BLM bonding on file isn't enough to plug all of
them, he says.
With more than 11,000 inactive wells on federal land, the agency hasn't changed its bonding rates since 1960
-- from $10,000 a well to a blanket $150,000 for all a driller's wells nationwide -- even for inflation. Steven
Wells, chief of the BLM's fluid-minerals division, says the agency is considering proposing an increase.
At his ranch near Gillette, John Hines, a former Wyoming state Senate president, points out several of the
roughly 50 abandoned High Plains wells on his land. An earlier owner left pipes strewed around and cattle get
into the well huts. "Each one leaves it to the next one," he says. "The major companies sell it to companies
and individuals that don't really have the means to reclaim things."
Mr. Presley says he is sympathetic to landowners' frustrations.
He hopes to acquire more wells but doesn't expect to revive High Plains. "High Plains dies on the vine," he
says. "And all those collection efforts and everything, they die on the vine, too."

On Wednesday in the U.S., much of the focus was on Ms. Yellen's congressional testimony, but traders said
her comments didn't include any surprises.
In her Tuesday testimony, Ms. Yellen had indicated that the central bank is moving slowly toward raising

comments. On Wednesday, she repeated that message.
"It's quiet as people are still trying to process Janet Yellen's testimony over the last couple days and decide
how dovish it was," said Ian Winer, head of equities trading at Wedbush Securities.
Even as stocks have climbed to new highs in recent sessions, trading volumes have been low.
In January, large intraday swings in stocks were common in daily trading, often driven by moves in oil prices
and foreign currencies as well as concerns about Europe.

approach to eventual interest-rate increases.

index, which measures expectations for swings in the S&P 500 and is commonly considered a fear gauge of
financial markets, ended 1.1% higher at 13.84 for the day.
"If you're a mountain climber, as you get to the icier peaks, you have to go more slowly, that's why markets
tend to move higher much more carefully than they go lower," said David Kelly, chief global strategist for J.P.
Morgan Asset Management. He said he anticipated the grind higher will continue.
Among the sectors moving higher Wednesday were shares of biotechnology companies, a sector that has

period.

media report Wednesday that Pharmacyclics was considering a sale, as well as more broadly to the recent
news that Valeant Pharmaceuticals International will buy Salix Pharmaceuticals.

World News: Ukraine Currency Plunges, Signaling Unease --- The hryvnia's meltdown presents a new challenge to Kiev as it battles rebels in...
World News: Ukraine Currency Plunges, Signaling Unease --- The hryvnia's meltdown presents a new
challenge to Kiev as it battles rebels in the east
By Paul Sonne and Josie Cox
602 words
24 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Ukraine's central bank moved to calm panicky currency markets on Monday after the hryvnia plummeted to
a new low against the dollar, while the cease-fire in the country's east showed more signs of unraveling.
The hryvnia's meltdown illustrated how even if the nearly year-old conflict with Russia-backed rebels in
eastern Ukraine eases, the government faces another traumatic battle on the economic front -- one that has
only just begun.
The Ukrainian military delayed a scheduled pullback of heavy artillery in the conflict zone on Monday, citing
continued attacks by separatists on Ukrainian positions. The withdrawal from the front line was part of a
European-brokered peace plan reached on Feb. 12.
"First there's supposed to be a cessation of fire by both sides of the conflict, and after that the sides are to
pull back weapons and military equipment from the front line," Ukrainian military spokesman Anatoly
Stelmakh said in Kiev. "Seeing as the positions of Ukrainian troops are continuing to be shelled, it's too early
to talk about a withdrawal of military equipment."
Meanwhile, the central bank listed the official exchange rate as 28.3 hryvnia to the dollar by Monday
afternoon, down from 9 hryvnia to the dollar a year ago. The currency declined even further over the day, to
more than 30 hryvnia to the dollar, according to traders. It has slid more than 43% against the dollar so far
this year.
National Bank Gov. Valeria Gontareva moved to rein in the panic trading by banning banks from issuing loans
to companies for the purpose of buying foreign currency, and announcing limits on prepayments by importers.
"We at the National Bank still have other instruments in our arsenal," Ms. Gontareva said at a briefing. "We
hope that we can stabilize the situation using this instrument," she said, vowing to prevent the situation from
getting "out of control."
The central bank raised interest rates in early February and scrapped foreign-exchange auctions that
essentially served as a peg for the hryvnia, drastically weakening the currency.
The country's foreign-currency and gold reserves hit a 10-year low of $6.4 billion in January. The government
expects the economy to contract by 5.5% over the calendar year.
The International Monetary Fund and other donors this month announced a $40 billion emergency rescue
package over four years for Ukraine's economy, including $17.5 billion in IMF funds. As part of the rescue
program, the Ukrainian government expects to start restructuring talks next month with its foreign
debtholders, including Russia.
Despite Monday's measures, analysts raised concerns about whether Ukraine would be able to avoid a
broader economic collapse. "The central bank has raised interest rates aggressively and the currency is still
weakening," said emerging-market strategist Piotr Matys at Rabobank in London.
"If it continues to weaken, this might lead to full-scale panic, which could eventually even lead to the central
bank having to implement capital controls in the worst-case scenario," he said. "At the moment it is very
difficult to be optimistic."
Timothy Ash, an analyst at Standard Bank , noted that Ukraine's budget this year is based on an exchange
rate of 21.7 hryvnia to the dollar, raising an array of challenges if the currency continues to weaken.
"Let's not beat around the bush -- Ukraine is facing economic/financial meltdown," Mr. Ash said in a note.

Gold prices fell, as worries eased about the eurozone bailout for Greece, driving investors out of the haven

officials.

Minister Hans Jorg Schelling said.

year. Gold tends to fare poorly against yield-bearing investments when borrowing costs rise. The yellow
metal declined 1.8% on the week and is down four consecutive weeks.
The market's attention will now likely turn back to the Fed, with Chairwoman Janet Yellen scheduled to give
monetary-policy testimony to Congress next week, said Bart Melek, head of commodities strategy at TD
Securities.
Indications that the Fed is in no hurry to raise rates are likely to push gold prices higher, Mr. Melek said in a
note to clients. He expects prices to reach $1,229 an ounce by the middle of the month.
"But there will be volatility, with prices moving . . . depending on economic data," he said. Strong U.S. data
would force gold prices lower, while weak numbers would have the opposite effect, he said.

REVIEW --- Books: The Creature Inside Her Head
REVIEW --- Books: The Creature Inside Her Head
By Karin Altenberg
1,044 words
21 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
H Is for Hawk
By Helen Macdonald
Grove, 300 pages, $26
Grief, as Helen Macdonald explains in "H Is for Hawk," is personal -- the pain is not to be shared. "It happens
to everyone," she writes. "But you feel it alone." Yet in this remarkable, fraught book, she tries to convey her
grief and mourning after her father's sudden death. To categorize this work as merely memoir, nature writing
or spiritual writing would understate her achievement.
Shortly after her father's death, Ms. Macdonald, already a keen falconer, drives from Cambridge, England, to
a Scottish quay to buy a young goshawk from an Irishman just off the ferry from Belfast. As grief creates a
gulf between Ms. Macdonald and other people, she becomes fixated on her task of taming the hawk, Mabel.
Slowly the author loses her moorings and drifts away from everything: her job, her house, her bills, her
friends, her self. Making the jesses, the leather straps that tether the hawk, she is trying to hold on, not letting
go. She is trying to pull the goshawk into her own dislocated world.
But Mabel is very much her own creature: "She is a conjuring trick. A reptile. A fallen angel. A griffon from the
pages of an illustrated bestiary. Something bright and distant, like gold falling through water. A broken
marionette of wings, legs and light-splashed feathers." The goshawk seems ancient, like a dinosaur, and her
instinct is to kill. In yarak -- the falconry term describing a raptor in the mood to slay -- she is murderous,
frightful. At other times she is amazingly playful -- peeking into a paper telescope or playing throw-and-catch
with paper balls. She even recognizes pictures of pheasants.
Slowly, as the training of the hawk progresses, time and space disappear and the worlds of the woman and
the bird seem close -- like watercolors merging in a child's drawing. Revisiting her early life, Ms. Macdonald
draws a picture of a solitary little girl: tall, gawky, willing herself to be invisible, to be a watcher like her
photojournalist father. Searching for herself in the natural rather than the human world, she "made herself
disappear, and then, in the birds she watched, took flight." Halved at birth, when her twin brother died, Ms.
Macdonald is halved again at the death of her father.
Ms. Macdonald has already published one wonderful book ("Falcon") on the cultural history of falcons and
falconry, and throughout this volume she adds more detail on the subject. I was fascinated to learn of terms
from medieval falconry that survive in modern English -- "fed up," for example, describes a bird that has
eaten to much to hunt and is torpid and indifferent. And to "rouse" is quite simply to shake one's feathers. But
the bulk of the book is an investigation of Ms. MacDonald's own biography, and the emotional inwardness
and solitariness of Ms. Macdonald's account throw the narrative into a realm that is personal and therefore
difficult to fully share. At times, the reader may feel excluded from her emotions. But it is through the attempt
to explain them, rather than show them, that Ms. Macdonald -- counterintuitively perhaps -- manages to
implicate the reader.
Also woven into the structure of "H Is for Hawk" is a biography, of sorts, of the author T.H. White (1906-64).
Best known for retelling the Arthurian legend in "The Once and Future King," White was also the author of
"The Goshawk," a book that describes White's own training of a goshawk while working as a schoolmaster at
Stowe, a boarding school in Buckinghamshire. Ms. Macdonald read "The Goshawk" as a child and returns to
it while training Mabel. There is symmetry between White's interest in taming the hawk and her story. "He had
refused humanity in favour of hawks, " she writes, but he could not escape himself."
I can see the fascination White holds for her, but are these passages really necessary to the fabric of Ms.
Macdonald's book? I don't think so. I discern something of Macdonald the scholar behind the White
passages, and I want more of Macdonald the writer, because her prose glows and burns: "Vast flocks of

--- Page 37 ---
Page 37 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
fieldfares netted the sky," she writes at one point, "turning it to something strangely like a sixteenth-century
sleeve sewn with pearls."
As Mabel learns to hunt -- learns what she is for -- Ms. Macdonald's writing soars: "Now the rabbit is dead, its
pelt bunched between the hawk's gripping talons, but blood upwells as she breaks into its chest, and I cannot
stop watching it, this horrible, mesmerising, seeping claret filling up the space, growing jelly-like as it meets
the air, like a thing alive. It was a thing alive." Hunting, Ms. Macdonald argues, makes you an animal, but the
death of an animal makes you human again. And it is at this point, when Mabel finds her true purpose, that
the author begins to return to the human world. In the end she finds her way back by recounting the moments
and places in the landscape that she shared with Mabel.
Helen Macdonald at her best -- writing on landscape and nature -- earns her place firmly in a long line of
(mostly male) nature writers: Barry Lopez, J.A. Baker, Robert Macfarlane, Richard Mabey. If there was any
lingering sense that women could not be part of the hunt -- that they should stay by the hearth -- she blows it
out the window. On the book's first page she describes an urge that is human rather than gendered -- the
pressing need to get out of the domesticated world and into the wild. "I felt odd: overtired, overwrought,
unpleasantly like my brain had been removed and my skull stuffed with something like microwaved aluminium
foil, dinted, charred and shorting with sparks. Nnngh. Must get out."
By the end of the book Ms. Macdonald finds that she and Mabel are part of the same wide world, where they
share their "lives happily in all their separation." H is for Hawk. But H is, of course, also for Helen.
---
Ms. Altenberg is the author of the novel "Island of Wings."

Stocks rose, with the S&P 500 eking out a new record, as investors again kept an eye on talks between
Greece and its creditors.
The S&P 500 gained 3.35 points, or 0.2%, to 2100.34, its second record finish in as many sessions.

Overseas early Wednesday, Japan's Nikkei was up almost 1%, Australia's S&PASX 200 was up 0.7% and

In the U.S. on Tuesday, stocks spent the morning slightly lower before staging a modest rally through the
afternoon.

deal. The extension would prevent the country's current loan agreement from expiring at the end of the
month, easing fears among investors that the country could leave the eurozone and set off financial instability
across Europe.

said Brian Fenske, head of sales trading at ITG. "The markets are getting better at shrugging it off."
Shares of small companies, seen as better insulated from troubles overseas, also rose. The Russell 2000
index of small shares rose 1.88 points, or 0.2%, to a record of 1225.01.
Technology stocks, which have been leading the broader market higher in recent months, lagged behind
Tuesday. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 5.43, or 0.1%, to 4899.27.
After a January slump, U.S. stocks have recovered with the help of better news on the U.S. economy and as
fourth-quarter corporate earnings surpassed low early expectations. So far this year, the Dow is up 1.3%
while the S&P 500 has advanced 2%.
Going into the fourth-quarter earnings-reporting season, analysts polled by FactSet had expected profit at
S&P 500 companies to rise only 1.1% from a year earlier amid worries over the impact of a stronger dollar,
the slide in oil prices and weak economic growth overseas.
Including results from 396 companies of the S&P 500, profits are now on track to rise 4.1% from a year ago.
"Stocks are a still a very attractive place to be," said Hank Smith, chief investment officer at Haverford Trust,
which has about $8.2 billion under management. His bullish view stems in part from an expectation that the
economy will continue to recover, leading to better corporate earnings, he said.
Others say lofty valuations on U.S. stocks make them uneasy. Brendan Connaughton, chief investment
officer at ClearPath Capital Partners in San Francisco, said he has been shifting investments into European
stocks, in large part because they are more attractively priced than shares in the U.S. following years of
gains.
European stocks ended the day mixed after opening lower. France's CAC-40 rose 0.04% while Germany's
DAX fell 0.3%.

Page 39 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The U.S. economic calendar was light Tuesday. On Wednesday, investors are expected to pore over minutes
from the Federal Reserve's latest policy meeting for indications on the central bank's timing for raising
interest rates. A rate increase is widely expected later this year.
The 10-year Treasury note's yield rose slightly to 2.141%, compared with 2.019% on Friday. Bond yields

MANSION --- Foreign Correspondent: A Beach Town for Spotting Animals -- and Celebrities --- Australia's Byron Bay mixes sandy shores and...
MANSION --- Foreign Correspondent: A Beach Town for Spotting Animals -- and Celebrities ---
Australia's Byron Bay mixes sandy shores and hinterlands; city slickers and hippies
By Alyssa Abkowitz
774 words
13 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
M3
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In this seaside town, grazing animals lumber near multimillion-dollar beachfront properties. Celebrities relax
alongside surfers and backpackers. Byron Bay, about two hours south of Brisbane on the sunny coast of
eastern Australia, is a place of contrasts.
"One of my best friends is a yoga teacher and another good friend is a TV celebrity," says Ron Wages, a
retired electronics-business owner from the U.S. who bought a $1.2 million home in Byron Bay two years ago
and now lives there full time. Mr. Wages is among a growing group of home buyers wooed to the small town
-- Byron Bay is just 9,400 people, while the surrounding areas that make up the Byron Shire add an additional
20,000.
Actress and singer Olivia Newton-John runs a retreat and spa here and has owned a farm in the area since
1980. "It has the best of everything -- beaches, mountains, beautiful little villages," she says in an email.
According to local real-estate agents, Aussie actor Chris Hemsworth, better known as Thor in the superhero
movie series, spent about $5.4 million in September on an eight-bedroom, 11-bathroom home near Byron
Bay. Mr. Hemsworth's representative didn't respond to requests for comment.
A hippie-chic locale that mixes art fairs and farmers markets with fine dining, Byron Bay attracts a less flashy,
if affluent, crowd. "There's a very simple lifestyle," says Graham Dunn, a real-estate agent who has lived here
for 25 years. "It's more about dressing down than dressing up."
While many homeowners buy property as vacation homes, real-estate agents say more people are moving
here full time because of Byron Bay's geographical setup. It's only 45 minutes from the Gold Coast airport
and close to Brisbane, allowing executives to get in and out relatively quickly. And yet the area feels secluded
and remote, with rivers on its northern and southern sides, hinterlands to the west and the ocean on the east.
The lighthouse at Cape Byron stands on the most easterly point of all of Australia.
Another appeal for many homeowners is the town's aversion to density. Commercial buildings can't be taller
than three stories and residential heights are capped at two. Properties with less than 100 acres can't be
subdivided, leaving "a lot of land that won't ever be developed," says luxury real-estate agent Nicolette van
Wijngaarden.
One of the newest projects under way -- the first in-town residential development in about 20 years, according
to local real-estate agents -- is Fortyfive Lawson, a beachfront complex that will have nine apartments ranging
in price from about $2.3 million to $3.8 million. Mr. Dunn, the exclusive listing agent of the development, says
sales began in November 2013, and one unit is left.
The market is picking up, following the recent strength of the national real-estate market. Mr. Dunn's
real-estate firm, for example, has about 40 listings, compared with 142 in the fall of 2013. The median
single-family home price in Byron Bay was about $513,000 at the end of 2014, up 21% from December 2013,
according to Andrew Wilson, senior economist at the Domain Group, a real-estate website in Australia.
Living here seems to have a few downsides. Residents say traffic in the township is horrendous during peak
times, which include Christmas, Easter and music-festival weekends.
Property developer Greg Crane and his wife, Philippa, have been coming to Byron Bay since the 1980s. They
recently decided to take advantage of low interest rates, buying a $4.5 million home in the exclusive
Wategos community in May. The home, which locals call the Wing House because of its contemporary
design with a sweeping roof, was originally on the market for around $5.4 million. The steel, timber, concrete
and glass home includes an ionized lap pool, 23-foot-high curved ceilings and terraced gardens.
After traveling through Byron Bay during a motorcycle trip when he was 17, Ned Rockman, now 45, knew he
wanted to get back to the area someday. In June 2013, Mr. Rockman, who used to run an organic-foods

--- Page 41 ---
Page 41 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
store in Melbourne, bought a four-bedroom, three-bathroom home in Newrybar, surrounded by plantations
and farmland with views of the ocean, for $1.4 million.
"When I have coffee on the veranda I pinch myself," Mr. Rockman says. "You can't get sick of the views."

Gold Demand Takes a Break --- Global Total for 2014 Is Pulled Down by Tumble in China's and India's Markets
Commodities Report
Gold Demand Takes a Break --- Global Total for 2014 Is Pulled Down by Tumble in China's and India's
Markets
By Ese Erheriene
652 words
13 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Global demand for gold fell last year as buying plunged in two important markets, China and India, the World
Gold Council said Thursday.
Total demand in 2014 weighed in at 3,924 tons, compared with 4,088 tons the previous year. However,
growth in demand increased into the end of the year: Fourth-quarter demand was 988 tons, up 6% from the
year-earlier quarter.
Last year "was a year of stabilization . . . after the record-breaking level of buying seen in 2013," said Marcus
Grubb, managing director of investment strategy at the World Gold Council.
The market still is recovering from 2013, when the largest annual decline in the price of gold in more than 30
years sparked a surge in demand, particularly from Indian and Chinese buyers in search of a bargain. As a
result, demand in 2013 was a record, according to the WGC.
On Thursday, the most actively traded gold contract inched up 0.1% to $1,220.70 an ounce on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. After posting a 1.5% loss last year, gold is up 3.1% so far in
2015 amid worries about global growth, the conflict in Ukraine and concerns about the sluggish eurozone
economy.
The strengthening U.S. economy and dollar has made the precious metal more expensive for investors
holding other currencies, the WGC said.
The two Asian countries are the biggest consumers of gold and together make up roughly half of global
demand. But purchases were down sharply last year, falling 14% in India to 843 tons and a whopping 38% to
814 tons in China.
There was some divergence within the different categories. Jewelry demand, which accounts for about
two-thirds of total demand, was down 10% last year.
But it was up 19% in India, where Indians splurged on items for festivals and weddings.
Chinese jewelry demand, however, fell by about a third. The WGC said a surge in consumption there in 2013
exhausted demand, leading to the slump last year.
The WGC expects gold demand from India and China to increase in 2015. It estimates consumption in each
of those countries will total around 900 to 1,000 tons.
Of the major consuming economies, only the U.K. and France registered total demand growth for the year, up
18% and 16%, respectively, according to the report. In the U.K., steps such as the Royal Mint's recent
issuance of its first gold-bullion coins for sale to the public have increased the popularity of owning the metal.
"Before 2007, the European bar and coin market was pretty much nonexistent," said Alistair Hewitt, head of
market intelligence at the World Gold Council, in an interview. But since the 2008 financial crisis, Europeans
have become big fans, Mr. Hewitt said, and consumption "now ranks [Europe] alongside India and China."
Meanwhile, central banks snapped up net purchases of 477 tons of gold over the year, up 17% from 2013.
Russia's central bank led the way, followed by Iraq and Kazakhstan, suggesting that the appetite of
emerging-market countries for storing money in the traditional haven metal to prevent against economic
shocks hadn't diminished, according to the WGC.

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
However, after a turbulent year, Ukraine sold almost 19 tons of its central reserve gold holdings in 2014, for a
decline of 44%. The country has been fighting Russian-backed separatists in the southeastern part of the
country.
Mine supply, recycling, producer hedging and other aspects of the overall supply of gold to the market shrank
2% in 2014 to 1,091 tons, from 1,113 tons the year before. But mine output hit a record of 3,114 tons, rising
2%, according to the WGC. This is expected to plateau in 2015.
