REVIEW --- Books of the Year: 12 Months of Reading --- We asked 50 of our friends -- from Lena Dunham to Paul Ryan -- to name their favorite...
REVIEW --- Books of the Year: 12 Months of Reading --- We asked 50 of our friends -- from Lena
Dunham to Paul Ryan -- to name their favorite books of 2014
10,616 words
13 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Karen Abbott
Gary Krist's "Empire of Sin" had me at the subtitle -- who could resist a tale that promises sex, jazz and
murder in New Orleans? -- and the (true) narrative brilliantly delivers. Mr. Krist guides us through a deliciously
ribald time in Crescent City history, from the end of the Gilded Age to the dawn of Prohibition, when the city's
elite battled its prodigious and flamboyant underworld. And I've never read anything quite like Walter Kirn's
"Blood Will Out," a gorgeously strange hybrid of memoir and true-crime potboiler that recounts his 15-year
friendship with "the most prodigious serial impostor in recent history": Christian Karl Gerhartsreiter, otherwise
known as Clark Rockefeller. As Mr. Kirn traces their relationship, culminating in Gerhartsreiter's trial for a
grisly 1985 murder, he examines the mercurial nature of the past and the ways in which we're all capable of
deceiving -- not only others but also ourselves.
-- Ms. Abbott's most recent book is "Liar, Temptress, Soldier, Spy."
Sophia Amoruso
Innovation. Startup culture. Business building. We hear these words every day, and yet there is little
rationality in true entrepreneurialism. In "The Hard Thing About Hard Things," Ben Horowitz delivers terrific
business advice that isn't taught in the classroom. In "Not That Kind of Girl," Lena Dunham brilliantly explores
the essence of growing up and what it takes to get through heartbreak, loneliness and plaguing insecurities.
Ultimately, she arrives at a newfound sense of self. A singular voice that has touched so many of us, Ms.
Dunham has helped define popular culture as we know it.
-- Ms. Amoruso is the CEO of Nasty Gal and the author of "#GIRLBOSS."
Kristen Anderson-Lopez and Robert Lopez
Hands down, Kristen's favorite pick this year was "All the Light We Cannot See" by Anthony Doerr. At once a
carefully researched window into a single moment on a single island during World War II, it is also an epic
tale woven from converging lives. You fall in love with the two protagonists, who, as experienced through
slices of their lives big and small, are victims of time and place. Along the way, you learn the science of
modern radio, the tactics of the French Resistance, the geological origin of diamonds and the feel of a single
snail shell to a blind child. Robert's favorite was "Creativity, Inc." by Ed Catmull, a book about how the minds
at Pixar were able not only to harness incredible creativity and innovation for their first film, "Toy Story," but to
create an organizational structure that could cultivate those qualities for years to come. The secret, Mr.
Catmull reveals, lies in not flinching from, and in fact embracing, the inevitable failures that occur during the
development of any project. Any artist who struggles daily with this issue will find this book deeply comforting,
not to mention validating. Finally, books for the kids: Our 9-year-old read R.J. Palacio's "Wonder" several
times back-to-back and could not stop taking about it this summer. It's a seemingly young-adult story about a
boy named Auggie with a deformed face who enters middle school. But it is so beautifully executed, and so
profoundly universal, that it is the kind of book that can change the world. And "The Book With No Pictures" is
a work of elegant subversion, perpetrated by B.J. Novak upon adults to make them look silly in front of young
children, who then erupt in joyful screaming and laughing mockery of them.
-- Ms. Anderson-Lopez and Mr. Lopez wrote the songs for the movie "Frozen."
Kathryn Bigelow
This year, "The Sixth Extinction" by Elizabeth Kolbert and "This Changes Everything" by Naomi Klein affected
me personally. Ms. Klein's own words say it most eloquently: "We are the first generation to witness climate
change and we are the last generation that can do something about it." I also read "The Unwinding" -- I have
been, and will always be, a fan of George Packer's work.

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- Ms. Bigelow is the director of "The Hurt Locker" and "Zero Dark Thirty."
Anthony Bourdain
I love Gabrielle Hamilton. I love her restaurant. I love her memoir, "Blood, Bones & Butter." I want to be her
when I grow up. Her latest, "Prune," is the anticookbook -- a smart, unpretentious and genre-smashing
departure from convention. Delicious, attainable, real-world cooking. I'm about halfway through Denis
Johnson's "The Laughing Monsters," a dark and darkly humorous satire/adventure story set in some of the
more violent, surreal parts of the Democratic Republic of the Congo, Liberia and other places where
everything is fine -- until, suddenly, it isn't. Finally, James Risen's "Pay Any Price" is an unflinching
examination of exactly where our money went in the various wars on terror. Reading about the crackpots,
opportunists and fantasists feasting at our national trough would be hilarious if it was a Coen Brothers film
instead of reality.
-- Mr. Bourdain is the host of CNN's "Anthony Bourdain: Parts Unknown."
Laura Bush
Among my most tender remembrances of my father are the airmail letters he posted as a World War II GI,
pages of transparent paper covered in his precise script. The Greatest Generation was a letter-writing
generation, rare in our electronic age. Now, my husband, George, has written a love letter to his dad, George
H.W. Bush, "41: A Portrait of My Father." It draws on memories and, yes, notes and letters. I knew George
H.W. Bush's beautiful mother, Dorothy Walker Bush, and I laughed when I read her lessons in humility for her
second son. When, as a boy, he explained losing at tennis because he was off his game, his mother replied:
"You don't have a game. If you work harder, maybe you'll get one." That son became a gracious and gentle
man, emailing his vice presidential opponent, Geraldine Ferraro, near the end of her life, "I hope you know
that I consider you a real friend. In fact, I hope it's okay if I say I love you." The last chapter of "41" makes me
cry. America's first chapter as a nation was written by many statesmen, and usually all we read are their
letters, but Myron Magnet reads their houses in "The Founders at Home." As someone who has lived in two
historic residences, the Texas Governor's Mansion and the White House, I am fascinated by how the
Founders' ideas were represented through architecture, by what was preserved for posterity and what was
disturbed. Mr. Magnet describes visiting James Madison's home amid a restoration: "The experience was like
being at the excavation of Pompeii, breathlessly watching a long-lost past come back to light." And if the polar
vortex returns, I'm looking forward to reading two new novels, "All the Light We Cannot See" by Anthony
Doerr and Colm Toibin's "Nora Webster."
-- Mrs. Bush is the former First Lady of the United States.
Caleb Crain
"It's amazing how well the veteran mystique plays," warns a veteran in "Redeployment," Phil Klay's sly, brutal
story collection. The mystique plays with redoubled force, it turns out, when the veteran lets you know that he
knows your cliched ideas about him. "I don't want you to respect what I've been through," a soldier tells a
civilian. "I want you to be disgusted." Is it right to repurpose moral ambivalence as a weapon of seduction? It's
hard to resist. Mr. Klay has made art out of weighty personal experience. At the other extreme, the
experience implied by Ben Lerner's novel "10:04" seems startlingly light. After publishing an autobiographical
short story in the New Yorker, a narrator named Ben Lerner lands an enviable book contract. Will he be able
to spin out his meringue-like, essayistic, self-mocking reflections at book length? Should he donate sperm to
a woman he might or might not like to have a child with? What should he watch while he does? "Arctic
Summer," Damon Galgut's fictional account of the romantic and sexual development of the novelist E.M.
Forster, is plainer in style, more sober in tone and more evidently painstaking -- like Forster. Mr. Galgut's
Forster is a virgin until 37, and his romantic nature emerges complexly entangled with Britain's fading power
in the Middle East and on the Subcontinent, culminating in his novel "A Passage to India," which Mr. Galgut
imagines to be a summa of what Forster learned about lust, shame, democracy, race and love.
-- Mr. Crain is the author of the novel "Necessary Errors."
Meryl Davis & Charlie White
This year Charlie stayed up all night reading "Red Rising," a sci-fi/fantasy book by Pierce Brown, with an
enthralling story about a lowly hero, born to toil underneath the surface of Mars, who rises up to fight against
oppression. It's exactly the type of book you want during a year full of training, competing and performance.
You have to keep reading to find out what happens next. Now Meryl, a big fan of classic mythology, is
reading the book too and loving how it incorporates the old world and the new.
-- Ms. Davis and Mr. White won a gold medal for ice dancing at the 2014 Olympics.
Peter Diamandis

--- Page 148 ---
Page 148 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
I'm devouring Eric Schmidt and Jonathan Rosenberg's "How Google Works." Over the past eight years, I've
had the pleasure of working with the senior leadership of Google and have picked up bits and pieces of the
company's mind-set. But having the practices laid out in such an intimate fashion is extremely useful; I've
given copies to all of my company executives as suggested reading. In the sci-fi realm, I loved reading
"Ready Player One" by Ernest Cline. The book was recommended to me by Elon Musk and Philip Rosedale.
An incredible adventure story filled with references to the technology being developed in the labs today,
"Ready Player One" paints a vibrant, believable picture of how we will live, play and interact in the decades
ahead.
-- Dr. Diamandis is the CEO of the XPRIZE Foundation and author of the forthcoming "Bold."
Emma Donoghue
I've always been a Joseph O'Connor fan but generally feel that his meaty historical fictions outrank those he
sets in the present day. Yet "The Thrill of It All," about the rise and fall of a rock band, is one of his most richly
satisfying books -- eloquent, moving, laugh-out-loud funny. I feel I can still somehow hear the (fictional)
songs. Lily King's "Euphoria" I recall like an illness or a drug trip. This novel about anthropological research
undertaken in 1930s Borneo by an entangled trio (inspired by Margaret Mead and two of her husbands) is not
only smart but pungently sensual. Finally, I didn't hear about Elizabeth Strout's "Olive Kitteridge" when it
came out; it took Frances McDormand's face on the paperback (from the new HBO miniseries) to win my
attention. But I can't remember being so consistently stunned by every story in a collection: life at its most
tragic, radiant, ordinary -- the whole world in a small Maine town.
-- Ms. Donoghue's most recent novel is "Frog Music."
Lena Dunham
If you go on Twitter right now and search the hashtag #feminism, your face will explode and your eyeballs will
be swallowed up by your brain. It's a fraught time to be female in America (or should I say fraught-er), and a
newly expanded edition of Rebecca Solnit's "Men Explain Things to Me" is the most clarifying, soothing and
socially aware document I've read on the topic this year. Not to mention funny, in that dry way women with
long and glamorous gray-flecked hair are funny. Chelsea Martin's "Even Though I Don't Miss You" is also
funny, and tragic in the way that staring at the Internet until you pass out in a pile of Doritos crumbs is tragic.
Her deceptively relaxed prose perfectly captures the Facebook-guzzling void that constitutes modern
heartbreak. Fav.
-- Ms. Dunham is the creator of the series "Girls" and the author of "Not That Kind of Girl."
Jonathan Eig
Usually, I read to escape. But this year, for reasons I can't explain, I fell most deeply under the spell of books
all too grounded in reality. I began with Phil Klay's "Redeployment," which redeploys the reader, taking him to
a place he's never been but needs to see: America's Iraq. These stories are funny, ferocious and
unforgettable. In "Pro," Katha Pollitt takes one of the great moral arguments of our time and challenges
conventional thinking. Given that women throughout history have routinely sought to end pregnancies, she
contends, the only true choice is between legal, safe abortions and illegal, unsafe ones. Any argument to the
contrary, Ms. Pollitt maintains, is rooted in superstition or misogyny. And I finished 2014 with "Station Eleven"
by Emily St. John Mandel, which tells the story of a troupe of Shakespearean actors roving a barren
countryside after a virus has ravaged the earth. With Ebola headlines in the news, I wasn't sure that I could
handle this, but it turned out to be beautiful and uplifting. I'll try to lighten up a little in 2015.
-- Mr. Eig's most recent book is "The Birth of the Pill."
Mohamed El-Erian
We have been spoiled this year when it comes to thought-provoking books on economics and business: from
Thomas Piketty's controversial tome on inequality to Walter Isaacson's masterful examination of innovators;
from Michael Lewis's account of high-frequency trading to Eric Schmidt and Jonathan Rosenberg's look into
how Google works; from Julia Angwin's investigation of daily surveillance to Atif Mian and Amir Sufi's
research on debt and its implications. In "Creativity, Inc." Ed Catmull provides a candid view of Pixar's long
struggle to become the best animation studio in the world. He brilliantly describes how the company
revolutionized an industry while maintaining its edge. In "The Second Machine Age," Erik Brynjolfsson and
Andrew McAfee brilliantly bring to life the technological innovations that are transforming both work and
homes. They take us through breathtaking changes to virtually everything we do and how we do it.
-- Mr. El-Erian is the former CEO of Pimco and the chief economic adviser at Allianz.
Joseph Epstein

--- Page 149 ---
Page 149 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
My great discovery during the past year has been the writing of Milton Steinberg, a rabbi at the Park Avenue
Synagogue famous in his day, who died in 1950 at the age of 46. Steinberg wrote an extraordinary historical
novel, "As a Driven Leaf," set in second-century Judea and Syria, about the struggle between faith and
reason in the life of a Jewish apostate named Elisha ben Abuyah. I also read Steinberg's fine primer "Basic
Judaism" and his "The Making of the Modern Jew." Milton Steinberg's especial talent was for bringing
splendid clarity to abstruse subjects. I read, and was much impressed by, "Rumor and Reflection" and
"Sunset and Twilight," the two volumes of the diaries of Bernard Berenson, which are filled with brilliant
apercus. "The Five," a novel by the Zionist politician Vladimir Jabotinsky, was the surprise of this year's
reading. Who knew that this fierce polemicist had the sensibility of a first-class literary man? I'm closing out
the year by reading, for the second and doubtless final time, "The History of the Decline and Fall of the
Roman Empire," surely the most ambitious and entertaining history ever written. That little 18th-century
pudge, Edward Gibbon, could really pick it.
So don't worry, Ma, I'm reading good.
-- Mr. Epstein's most recent book is "A Literary Education and Other Essays."
Lisa Fain
As a home cook my restaurant experience is limited, so it was thrilling to get a firsthand look at a busy
restaurant kitchen in Michael Gibney's "Sous Chef." The story is written in the second person, which makes
the stress, energy and joy of fast-paced restaurant life immediate and easily understood. I have also become
obsessed with Vietnamese food, and Andrea Nguyen's "The Banh Mi Handbook," is packed with serious
recipes for all the elements needed to compose these Vietnamese sandwiches, everything from bread and
pickles to sausage and head cheese. While my food reading took me to new places, fiction brought me closer
to home. James Lee Burke's "Wayfaring Stranger," a saga set in Texas in the first half of the 20th century, is
beautiful and lyrical. The author's descriptions of Texan fare such as tamales, tacos and fried chicken will
make you hungry.
-- Ms. Fain is the author of "The Homesick Texan's Family Table."
Bill Gates
Shortly after we met, Warren Buffett loaned me "Business Adventures" by John Brooks, a collection of New
Yorker business articles from the 1950s and 1960s. Brooks's insights about business have aged beautifully,
and they are as true today as ever. This neglected classic is still my favorite business book. Thomas Piketty's
"Capital in the Twenty-First Century" sparked a fantastic global discussion about inequality. The author kindly
spent an hour discussing his work with me. As I told him, although I have concerns about some of his
secondary points and policy prescriptions, I agree with his most important conclusions: Inequality is a growing
problem, and governments should play a role in reducing it. Finally, Graeme Simsion's novel "The Rosie
Effect" made me think about relationships: what makes them work and how we have to keep investing time
and energy to make them better. A sweet, entertaining and thought-provoking book.
-- Mr. Gates, co-chair of the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, shares more favorite books at
GatesNotes.com.
Daniel Handler
The world is strange, and my favorite literature always dwells in the essential oddity of being alive. Two
novels that really held me this year are Emily St. John Mandel's "Station Eleven" and John Darnielle's "Wolf in
White Van," both of which approach the speculative and even the apocalyptic with a genuine and smart
warmth -- which doesn't mean I didn't also find both books deliciously chilling. Also quasi-apocalyptic, but
amazingly not speculative, is Suki Kim's "Without You, There Is No Us: My Time With the Sons of North
Korea's Elite," which I devoured for its wry and rare observations on that inexplicable land. And if I were
dictator, the book I'd require for every citizen is "Citizen: An American Lyric," Claudia Rankine's book-length
poem that shines in a really shiny year for poetry. "Citizen" is thoughtful and fierce, generous and angry,
capturing in its wide, wise eye the strangeness of living in -- what's the word I'm looking for? -- America.
-- Mr. Handler writes under the pen name Lemony Snicket.
Paul Harding
Elizabeth McCracken is a national treasure, and her story collection "Thunderstruck" made me realize just
how much I'd been missing her fiction the past few years. The stories here are brilliant, funny and
heartbreaking. She has an eye like no other and the world, refracted through it onto the page, in what feels
like hyperreal prose but is always in fact, word for word, the most aesthetically sophisticated prose you can
imagine, shows up reshuffled, askew, exploded and utterly recognizable. She's aces, all the way. And I loved
Marcus Burke's novel "Team Seven," which is about a kid in a tough neighborhood outside of Boston, trying

--- Page 150 ---
Page 150 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to cope with his ne'er-do-well, smoked-out dad, his talent as a basketball player, dealing grass for a local
gang and all the usual perils of growing up. Deep and funny and hair-raising, it's been hailed as a fantastic
debut of African-American fiction, which it is, and it's a plain fantastic book, period.
-- Mr. Harding's latest novel is "Enon."
Stephen Harrigan
Texas is a perennial subject/target for nonfiction writers with a locked-andloaded thesis, usually about how
the state is either an insufferable outlier to our national identity or the unsettling embodiment of it. It's the
fiction writers, though, who tell us what it's like to actually live here. James Magnuson's "Famous Writers I
Have Known" is an acid-free satire about an MFA program suspiciously like the James Michener Center for
Writers at the University of Texas at Austin. (A suspcion that's easily confirmed, since Magnuson is the
director of the Michener Center.) Although it's meant to be a screwball academic comedy, it features a
poignant and beautifully etched portrait of Rex Schoeninger, a Michenerian novelist whose vast commercial
success has forever denied him the critical validation he covets. The Austin campus is also the locale for the
precise and harrowing beginning of Elizabeth Crook's "Monday Monday." An ordinary coed named Shelly
Maddox has just left class on an August day in 1966 and is deciding whether to have a Coke or a Sego diet
drink when Charles Whitman targets her from the top of the UT tower. The rest of the novel tracks the next 40
years of Shelly's life, matching the action of that unforgettable opening chapter with equally compelling
scenes of emotional turmoil and insight. Bret Anthony Johnston's "Remember Me Like This" is set in and
around my hometown of Corpus Christi, a land of Whataburgers and Shrimporees and dolphin rehabilitation
facilities. Like "Monday Monday," it begins in trauma, with a family in a sleepwalking state of dread, not
knowing whether the 11-year-old son who disappeared suddenly four years earlier will ever be found alive.
There is a compelling mystery at the heart of the novel, but it's a mystery that Mr. Johnston solves almost by
osmosis, never directly revealing exactly what happened to Justin Campell yet somehow satisfying a reader's
deeper curiosity about how such a shattered family could ever pull itself back together.
-- Mr. Harrigan's most recent book is "The Eye of the Mammoth."
Mark Helprin
For reasons unknown but to God, Americans like to devour cinder-block-size political memoirs written by
ghost hacks without style for pygmy statesmen without substance. Should President Obama delay his
apotheosis so as to manufacture such a tome, perhaps it will be called "Moi! Not Since Einstein!" And Hillary
Clinton's next doorstop, whether or not she makes it back to 1600 Pennsylvania Avenue: "Mein Kampf to
Regain the Throne." Even Henry Kissinger, brilliant of substance, is possessed of a style only a small helping
of which could stock a large cement works until the sun explodes. Rather than pay $35 to spend 1,000 pages
watching an egomaniac grind an ax, why not part with $21.95, as I did in the museum shop at Monticello, for
"The Adams-Jefferson Letters: The Complete Correspondence Between Thomas Jefferson and Abigail and
John Adams," covering 1777 to 1826. Two truly great statesmen and a wonderful woman write in confidence
and ofttimes under stress about the dangers and glories of the American Founding, its philosophical basis,
the early development of the republic, the rise of party, their personal tragedies, their hopes, disappointments
and observations of war, the courts of Europe, the American landscape, theology, the classics and their
famous contemporaries (did you know that, according to Adams, Washington was forced not to seek another
term?). In these letters, our second and third presidents, whose intellectual powers have not been surpassed
by their successors except perhaps by Abraham Lincoln, enchant the reader with prose (especially
Jefferson's) that is a pleasure to read for its rhythm and flow. Jefferson was profoundly radical, Adams
profoundly conservative, Abigail profoundly graceful, and to be brought close to them truly is a joy.
-- Mr. Helprin's most recent novel is "In Sunlight and in Shadow."
John Hickenlooper
It probably speaks to my history as a frustrated writer that I seek out successful writers as friends and
colleagues. "Soldier Girls" by Helen Thorpe is an example of the power of meticulous reporting and vivid
writing. Ms. Thorpe (my ex-wife and dear friend) weaves together the incredible stories of three women,
depicting the unique challenges that females in the military face while also illuminating the general challenges
our armed services confront in the 21st century. "Soldier Girls" is a critical piece of our history. Another
unforgettable read is "Shadows in the Vineyard" by Maximillian Potter (now one of my advisers). Mr. Potter's
book is in part a riveting narrative of an unprecedented crime committed against the world's most revered
winery, but the author harnesses this particularly crazy caper to entice non-oenophiles like me into a historical
epic that's rooted in a culture shaped by espionage, betrayal, romance, revolution and some mighty good
wine.
-- Mr. Hickenlooper is governor of Colorado.
Kaci Hickox

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Although this year seemed to be filled with oldies but goodies, at the top of my list of new books is Leah
Bassof and Laura DeLuca's "Lost Girl Found." Having read many books about the "lost boys" of Sudan, this
was a refreshing piece of fiction highlighting the struggles and triumphs of a young female Sudanese refugee.
Poni, the main character, describes her life of extremes, saying: "When I dance, I can jump out of my pain for
just a moment." I also read and was inspired by Sheryl Sandberg's "Lean In." Her simple but powerful
demand ("So please ask yourself: What would I do if I weren't afraid? And then go do it") continues to
challenge me. I spent a good portion of the year torn by the events and lives captured in Gore Vidal's
"Lincoln." Vidal's captivating account of the Civil War and Lincoln's leadership during one of America's most
challenging times is one of my very favorite books.
-- Ms. Hickox is a nurse who treated Ebola patients in Sierra Leone.
Larry Hogan
I have to be honest. Over the past year, as I crisscrossed the state of Maryland campaigning to be the next
governor, I barely had enough time to read a restaurant menu, let alone a book. There is one, though, for
which I made time: "Things That Matter," a collection of writings by Pulitzer Prize-winning political
commentator, psychiatrist and Maryland resident Charles Krauthammer. The book has no chronological
order, so you can open it at any point and select one of the 88 newspaper columns or magazine essays at
your leisure. Perfect for the busiest of readers. Mr. Krauthammer's personal story is itself humbling. A diving
accident at a swimming pool on the grounds of Harvard Medical School, where he was a student, left him
paralyzed, but he refused to let this terrible accident define his life. "Things That Matter" is a collection of this
great thinker's very best essays -- his reflections on everything from his favorite sport (baseball), swearing in
public and the nuances of chess to the "golden age" of the 1990s and America's place in the world.
-- Mr. Hogan is governor-elect of Maryland.
Phil Klay
At a certain point, reading Matthew Thomas's novel "We Are Not Ourselves," I started canceling plans so I
could do nothing but read it. The story, which Mr. Thomas spent 10 years writing, covers three generations of
an Irish family. The author's eye is unflinching but never unsympathetic; this is a work of incredible insight and
power. Though Scott Cheshire is not religious, having left the Jehovah's Witnesses in his late adolescence,
his novel "High as the Horses' Bridles" approaches questions of faith with the same passion as a serious
believer. Revolving around a character who, after delivering an apocalyptic sermon as a child, leaves the
church but returns home to care for his ailing father, it's a searching and provocative addition to the tradition
of American religious novels. Elizabeth Samet, the author of "No Man's Land," teaches English literature at
West Point. Over the years she has seen her students graduate, deploy to war, return home, deploy again,
return again, deploy again and sometimes never return. This book is her answer to the question of how to
prepare students for the strange place of the military in relation to the wars it fights and the society it fights
for.
-- Mr. Klay is the author of "Redeployment."
Hermione Lee
The outstanding novel of the year for me was Colm Toibin's "Nora Webster," a subtle, magnetic, beautifully
voiced and profoundly moving story of an Irishwoman and her sons surviving and coming to terms with
bereavement, in a context of 1950s Irish small-town surveillance. Perhaps his best book yet -- and that's
saying something. I greatly enjoyed, and greatly learned from, two remarkable group, or "crowd," biographies.
The historian and biographer R.F. Foster's "Vivid Faces: The Revolutionary Generation in Ireland,
1890-1923" brilliantly and vigorously reveals the personal histories that shaped the Irish revolutionary
moment of the early 20th century. Jenny Uglow, whose previous subjects include the Lunar Men, Hogarth
and Gaskell, gives a masterly, broad-sweep description in "In These Times: Living in Britain Through
Napoleon's Wars, 1793-1815," which also succeeds in being tenderly, intimately particular about individual
life-stories.
-- Ms. Lee's most recent book is "Penelope Fitzgerald: A Life."
Monica Lewinsky
Binge reading? Yes. You lock the doors, cancel all appointments, order in and fall in love with a book. And if
that book is a 600-plus-page tome, and you actually fret when you hit the middle and realize that there are
only 300 pages left, well . . . such is "I Am Pilgrim," by Terry Hayes. Written in a heart-stopping pace, this
literary thriller lands somewhere between "Homeland" and "Breaking Bad" and then transports you to a whole
different level. It is part spy novel, part psychological thriller. Twists and turns. Emotionally complex
characters. Relevant geopolitical issues. I picked up the book in London, where it was recommended to me
and came out a year before the American pub date. Bringing it back to New York, I badgered everyone I

--- Page 152 ---
Page 152 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
knew: "You must read this book." Only one friend didn't finish it, because there is a fair amount of gruesome
violence. Another favorite book of the year was my dear friend Alan Cumming's brave and heartbreaking
memoir on his complicated paternal relationship, identity and survival. "Not My Father's Son" is an important
read particularly because of the issues of alienation that a LGBTQ youth -- or anyone, really -- who is bullied
may feel and face. Lastly, Courtney Watson McCarthy's "Gaudi Pop-Ups" is a magical book I discovered this
year: "Visual" books, particularly one about such a master, spark creativity. I loved it.
-- Ms. Lewinsky is an antibullying advocate, public speaker and contributor to Vanity Fair.
Francis Mallmann
Jorge Luis Borges, lecturing at Harvard in 1967, remarked that a book is a set of dead symbols that only
come to life in the hands of the reader. Peter Mendelsund's books "Cover" and "What We See When We
Read" make us understand how we construct these images in our minds, based on the words and dreams of
the writer. These are masterpieces touched not only by language but by Mr. Mendelsund's erudite use of
images, time, imagination and memory. The author tours us through literature, from how we physically
imagine Tolstoy's Anna Karenina to how Agatha Christie's writing constructs our vision of Inspector Hercule
Poirot. When we read, as when we gaze out of a car window, we use our eyes in an automatic way -- are we
really looking thoroughly at those mountains? For me, as I grow older, words and languages have become
my favored companions; they are always with me, and they take up no space.
-- Mr. Mallmann is a chef and the author of "Mallmann on Fire."
Emily St. John Mandel
"Wittgenstein Jr." is Lars Iyer's fourth book. His elegant novels, published by a small press, are slim only in
the sense of a limited page count. "Wittgenstein Jr." concerns a troubled lecturer in philosophy at Cambridge
and the somewhat baffled small group of students who attend his classes. The story that emerges is funny,
beautifully written, profound and unexpectedly moving. Elena Mauli Shapiro's "In the Red" is the book I can't
stop thinking about, and it hasn't gotten much attention this year. It is a dark, unsettling meditation on crime,
money, love and belonging, told from the perspective of Irina, who abandons college to attach herself to a
charismatic gangster. Her descent into amorality is fascinating and beautifully executed. "In the Red" is
brilliant, and that's not a word I use lightly.
-- Ms. Mandel is the author, most recently, of "Station Eleven."
Mike Matheny
For me, reading is an active process; I highlight books extensively and fill the margins with notes. After I
finish, I go back through a second time and transfer the thoughts into a notebook. I am a firm believer that the
best leaders are both learners and readers. As a young manager, with less experience than most of my
counterparts, I know I have plenty to learn, and I am typically reading multiple books at the same time. While I
usually read about leadership development, I also enjoy biographies of successful sports figures and
catalysts in the corporate and military worlds. My standouts in 2014 were "Coach Wooden's Greatest Secret"
by Pat Williams, "Fight" by Craig Groeschel and "Leaders Who Last" by Dave Kraft. These great reads
challenged me in every area of my life and helped me become a better coach, husband, father and friend.
-- Mr. Matheny is manager of the St. Louis Cardinals and the author of the upcoming "The Matheny
Manifesto."
Stanley McChrystal
For me, 2014 was a year to think about thinking. Daniel J. Levitin's fascinating analysis of how we think in
"The Organized Mind" provides a scientific foundation for how we deal with the increasing complexity of the
information age. In "My Promised Land," Ari Shavit took me where the science stops and into how our
attitudes are shaped not only by what we learn, what we see and how we feel but also by the experiences of
our parents, and their parents, as he provides a sometimes unfamiliar view into the birth and maturation of
Israel. Finally, in "World Order," Henry Kissinger flies to 30,000 feet to deliver a concise, but still sweeping,
history lesson and a valuable framework for the major foreign-policy issues of our day.
-- Mr. McChrystal is a retired U.S. Army four-star general.
Deirdre McCloskey
I read, or at least use, a lot of books to write my own. Twenty or so a week when I get going. For both work
and pleasure, though, I don't read what everyone else reads -- I find it makes you a New York Times
repeater. I avoid best sellers. We'll know in a century whether they're actually worth reading. The closest to a
best seller I've read with pleasure and profit this year is William Easterly's newest, "The Tyranny of Experts."

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For the rest I'd recommend a mix of obscure academic books and persuasive libertarian rants a la Rand Paul.
The best academic book I've read would be Michael Tomasello's "A Natural History of Human Thinking,"
which shows how metaphors and stories govern us from the get-go. Also academic, and unintendedly
libertarian in result, is Margaret Jacob's "The First Knowledge Economy." She shows, against her own
politics, how loony were French attempts to regulate innovation -- three centuries ago. Tom W. Bell goes after
the lunacy of our copyright law in "Intellectual Privilege: Copyright, Common Law, and the Common Good,"
and Tom G. Palmer, editor of "Peace, Love & Liberty," goes after the lunacy of our foreign policy. Enough
with lunacy already. And of course, to speak of pleasure reading, I doted on Donna Leon's latest mystery set
in my beloved Venice (Italy, not California), "By Its Cover." She seems even to be drifting toward
libertarianism, which anyway has always been implied by her understanding of the Italian government's
corruption and incompetence. A book I definitely did not like or learn from was Thomas Piketty's "Capital in
the Twenty-First Century." It shows how bad can be the test of best selling.
-- Ms. McCloskey's "Bourgeois Equality," the last in her Bourgeois Era trilogy, will be out next year.
Nico Muhly
I've made a pact with two friends to read those Karl Ove Knausgaard books -- "My Struggle." They are
spellbinding. The Norwegian writer turns the same obsessive lens through which he views the banalities of
his life toward the inside, revealing a deeply flawed and troubled narrator. He is a difficult and occasionally
loathsome person with whom to spend several hours, and yet I find myself stealing moments during the day
to advance just a few pages. At the end of each volume, we've promised one another a convivial meal:
something that never seems to happen in the books themselves. I recommend grabbing a friend and making
a similar pact.
-- Mr. Muhly is a composer living and working in New York.
John Mulaney
Let's start with happiness. "10% Happier" by Dan Harris is an amazing personal story of how Mr. Harris
learned to use meditation to calm the endless, distracting, sometimes menacing streams of thoughts that he
and all of us have. A memoir about Mr. Harris's fascinating career at ABC News would have been enough.
His description of how meditation helped him cope with the stress and vertigo of this career takes the book to
a whole other level. Now, on to friendship: the novel "Friendship" by Emily Gould. Life is messy and life in
New York is messy and expensive. Ms. Gould writes about maturity and money, among many other things, in
a thoughtful and hilarious way. Speaking of hilarious (and murder in Wisconsin), "No One Else Can Have
You" by Kathleen Hale is an excellent mystery set, coincidentally, in a town called "Friendship." Last but not
least, I was endlessly proud to watch my wife, Annamarie Tendler, write and publish her incredible make-up
book, "The Daily Face: 25 Make-Up Looks for Day, Night, and Everything in Between." The photographs are
fantastic, and her writing is sharp. Also our dog is in it.
-- Mr. Mulaney is the creator of the Fox sitcom "Mulaney."
Satya Nadella
It's been another year of eclectic reading for me, ranging from rereading "The History of the Peloponnesian
War" (Thucydides) to reading Thomas Piketty's monumental "Capital in the Twenty-First Century." But the
books that stood out for me were Daniel James Brown's "The Boys in the Boat" and three books on the
scarce commodity of our time . . . time itself. "The Boys in the Boat" is that ultimate celebration of life's
journey through the metaphor of a team sport. It's the shared dreams and individual perseverance, best
captured in those moments of "swing," that transform one's own journey through life. In my day job, I have
been reflecting a lot on what it means to live in a more connected and digital world. To me this is not about
the physical devices in our lives but the physiological benefits and challenges. "The Organized Mind" by
Daniel J. Levitin, "The End of Absence" by Michael Harris, and "Pressed for Time" by Judy Wajcman all bring
to light interesting perspectives on this increasingly relevant topic.
-- Mr. Nadella is the CEO of Microsoft.
Michael Oren
Living in what is frequently called a war zone, one might not be drawn to war books. Unless, of course, one
relishes the chance to read about someone else's war. Last year's monumental "The Guns at Last Light" by
master military historian Rick Atkinson is a hard work to top. Yet 2014 produced a close second -- ironically, a
book written in 1930 but only recently translated. "Fear" by Gabriel Chevallier, though described as a novel, is
a scarcely disguised memoir of one of the common French soldiers -- the poilus -- in the trenches of World
War I. Heroically, Chevallier presents the most unvarnished version of the horrors he experienced. "There
were the pieces of corpses," he wrote of the scene behind one rampart, "the shreds of bodies and clothes,
organs, severed members, and red and purple human flesh." A more sentimental war novel, "All the Light We

--- Page 154 ---
Page 154 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Cannot See" by Anthony Doerr traces the parallel lives of a blind French girl and a young German
wunderkind serving in the Wehrmacht. Mr. Doerr weaves their unconventional stories through the
all-too-commonplace agony of World War II and brings them to a stirring intersection.
-- Mr. Oren was Israel's ambassador to the U.S. from 2009 to 2013.
Tom Perkins
"The Literary Churchill" by Jonathan Rose is a most wonderful book for Churchill admirers. It is not in any way
a biography (the author assumes that the reader already knows about the great man's life) but a fascinating
study of how Churchill used the words of a number of treasured authors, playwrights and past statesmen
throughout his life to inform and influence the world. This possibly sounds boring, but the book sparkles and
is the best I have ever read on the man. "An Officer and a Spy" by Robert Harris is the inside story of the
Dreyfus Affair. Mr. Harris is one of my favorite authors, and I have read all his books, starting with "Selling
Hitler." Here his reconstruction of conversations all ring true, and the book is a page turner even though we all
are familiar with the final outcome. Finally, "The Shifts and the Shocks" by Martin Wolf is not a light read, but
it is a thorough look at the global economy following the shock of the popping of the Great Credit Bubble in
2008. Mr. Wolf will convince the reader that things are still a mess and that another similar shock can be
expected. His analysis of the structural weakness of the European Union is very convincing, and his
suspicion that the financial authorities will not do any better next time leaves the reader sadder but wiser.
-- Mr. Perkins is a venture capitalist, the author of the autobiography "Valley Boy" and a retired director of
News Corp.
Jayne Anne Phillips
My picks begin with "Something Rich and Strange: Selected Stories" by Ron Rash. These 30-odd stories
from the past 20 years, set in Appalachia from the Civil War to the Depression to today's rural meth epidemic,
are by turns chilling, funny, deeply empathic and always unforgettable. Mr. Rash is a genius finally
"discovered." Speaking of empathy, I loved Leslie Jamison's essays, "The Empathy Exams," and Frederick
Dillen's quirky novel "Beauty," in which a mergers-and-acquisitions professional learns that she's been fired
and buys a fish factory she was ordered to shut down, redefining her life in a bleak coastal hamlet. "The
Corpse Exhibition," writer-in-exile Hassan Blasim's stories of life in Iraq, is surreal and mind-blowing and
completely necessary. Helen Oyeyemi's "Boy, Snow, Bird," a powerful examination of race played out as lyric
metaphor, delivers the haunting power of myth. Poet Claudia Rankine's "Citizen: An American Lyric," a
revolutionary text written in short prose declarations, undermines the blinding soul massacre of everyday
racism in America with accessible, fierce perception, while Lisa Howorth's "Flying Shoes," a novel about an
eccentric Mississippi wife and mother who can't outdistance the unsolved childhood murder of her
stepbrother, crosses racial lines and questions the nature of love. Ditto Lily King's novel, "Euphoria"; Ms. King
invents her own characters and takes delicious, intellectually sound license with the real story of Margaret
Mead's 1933 field trip down New Guinea's Sepik River with her first husband and the man who would be her
second, Gregory Bateson. Ms. King transports us, while Louise Gluck, in her 10th book of poems, "Faithful
and Virtuous Night," achieves the arc of a novel with long, expansive lyrics and prose poems that are nearly
parables. Ms. Gluck's testament, that we emerge from darkness and return to it, shifting identities in a swirling
cosmos, invites the faith in language that lies at the exhilarating heart of reading.
-- Ms. Phillips is the author, most recently, of "Quiet Dell."
Zia Haider Rahman
David Grossman's "Falling Out of Time" broke my heart. Character and definition are pared back in this
harrowing exploration of loss, revealing the raw bone of grief. The Walking Man, the Woman, a Town
Chronicler, the Centaur and others, all shaped by sorrow, fold into a narrative of barely perceptibly widening
circles. By means of this concentric expansion the novel finds within the solitude of mourning the
companionship of suffering. And by drawing on techniques of poetry, playwriting and prose, Mr. Grossman
fashions a form to resonate with the substance. He has spoken of the death of his son, Uri, in 2006 and -- if it
is not too facile an observation -- the writing here bears the marks of personal tragedy.
I oscillate between loathing Vladimir Nabokov's novels (when I think he's showing off or insisting on making
his presence felt) and loving them. This morning, I can't stand a word of his fiction. But Nabokov's "Letters to
Vera" is not fiction. The showing off, such as there is, is that of a man to his lover, whose absence is felt. The
fact that these are the words -- such beautiful words -- of a writer to his beloved of more than 46 years
readies you for the spirit of sincerity in which they were written. Besides, I'm a sucker for a love story.
-- Mr. Rahman is the author of the novel "In the Light of What We Know."
Gina Raimondo

This was a busy year, but reading is a refuge for me and my favorite way to spend time with my kids, so even
during the busiest days I spent some time with a good book. "The Smartest Kids in the World (and How They
Got That Way)" by Amanda Ripley is a fascinating look at countries that are transforming their school
systems and personalizing student learning experiences. During the course of the campaign, I learned much
from "Give and Take" by Adam Grant on how investing in others' success can be the recipe for personal and
organizational success. Lastly, my son Tommy and I are reading "Who Was Amelia Earhart?" by Kate Boehm
Jerome, which explores Earhart's pioneering -- and exciting -- career.
-- Ms. Raimondo is governor-elect of Rhode Island.
Paul Ryan
Lately I've been reading about two founding fathers -- one real, one fictional. In "James Madison," Lynne
Cheney gives the historical portrayal of our fourth president a much-needed touch-up. Today we remember
Madison -- only 5-foot-6 and dressed in all black -- as quiet, bland and circumspect. But he was bold. He
helped organize the Constitutional Convention and made key proposals. In fact, some of those proposals, like
empowering the federal government to veto state laws, proved too bold for the delegates' taste. But Madison
shook off these defeats and took up the banner of ratification. The other founding father is Eli McCullough,
the patriarch of Philipp Meyer's novel "The Son." Born in 1836 on the Texas frontier, Eli is captured by
Comanches when he's only 13. His captors take him under their wing, and he grows to admire their rugged
way of life. He founds one of the great established families of Texas. Later on, his son, Peter, and his
great-granddaughter Jeanne Anne, agonize and puzzle over his bloody legacy. Both books explore how we
can learn from -- or live with -- the past.
-- Mr. Ryan represents Wisconsin's first congressional district and is chairman of the House Budget
Committee.
Bernie Sanders
We face two immense crises -- the decline of the American middle class and global warming. In "Losing Our
Way," Bob Herbert showcases his wonderful capacity to listen to Americans. He gives voice to real people's
stories, which highlight the decline of our middle class, the disappearance of millions of good-paying jobs, the
deterioration of our infrastructure and the dwindling opportunities for higher education at unaffordable
universities. Bill McKibben's "Oil and Honey" chronicles how he came to see that thinking and writing and
lecturing about global warming was not enough. He wanted to do something, to join a movement. When he
discovered there wasn't one, he and a small group of his students created 350.org, which made Mr.
McKibben one of the most successful organizers in America. He also spins a parallel narrative about working
at the local level, creating an apiary in Vermont. Messrs. Herbert and McKibben both recognize, rightly, that
the way forward will be through grass-roots activism.
-- Mr. Sanders is a U.S. senator from Vermont.
Howard Schultz
Gen. George Marshall was an extraordinary American and his insistence on doing the right thing in the face
of a partisan Congress is an example for us in our own time. He deserves to be better appreciated, though
Debi and Irwin Unger's "George Marshall: A Biography" is not as celebratory as I would have hoped. Forrest
Pogue and Ed Cray are the authoritative biographers, but I am a great fan of Greg Behrman's "The Most
Noble Adventure: The Marshall Plan and the Time When America Helped Save Europe," a story of vision,
leadership and incredible courage. Another favorite is Daniel James Brown's "The Boys in the Boat," an
unlikely story of a group of gritty, up-by-the-bootstraps young men pitted against the most elite and privileged
athletes the world could throw at them. But the book I'm recommending to everybody is "The Armed Services
Officer" (Department of Defense, 1950). My copy was given to me by Maj. Gen. Kenneth Dahl, one of the
finest military minds I know. A timeless, classic book of inspirational leadership, it praises "the will to take full
responsibility for a decision; the readiness to share its rewards with subordinates; the nerve to survive storm
and disappointment and to face toward each new day with the scoresheet wiped clean." As I wrote my own
book to celebrate our veterans, it was a reminder of the valor of the 2.5 million extraordinary men and women
that allows all Americans to enjoy our freedom. We need to honor the contributions, day in and day out, of
those who have volunteered to protect and preserve our republic.
-- Mr. Schultz is chairman and CEO of Starbucks and co-author of "For Love of Country."
Robert Shiller
"Stress Test" by Timothy Geithner is a substantive book from the former New York Fed president and
Treasury secretary, the man most in charge of government policy that prevented the recent financial crisis
from unraveling into another Great Depression. Particularly interesting are his descriptions of all the things
policymakers might have done but were constrained from doing. An important lesson from the book is that

--- Page 156 ---
Page 156 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
without great leadership we are still in danger of faulty responses to another such crisis. "Preventing
Regulatory Capture," edited by Daniel Carpenter and David A. Moss, is a volume of articles by a variety of
thinkers about how to preserve the integrity of government. It covers a central issue, for the most important
argument against regulation ever since Milton Friedman's 1962 classic, "Capitalism and Freedom," has been
that regulators are, one way or another, induced by the regulated to work for them instead of the public. "The
Butterfly Defect" by Ian Goldin and Mike Mariathasan takes its title from the phrase "the butterfly effect,"
coined in the 1960s by mathematician Edward Lorenz, a pioneer in the discipline called chaos theory. The
idea is that something as insignificant as a butterfly flapping its wings anywhere in the world could in principle
start a long sequence of effects that lead ultimately to a hurricane far away. Mr. Goldin points out that the
increasing interconnectedness of the world makes the world's economies, infrastructure, health and social
conditions behave like such an interconnected meteorological system. The next big crisis will be of
unexpected origin.
-- Mr. Shiller, a professor of economics at Yale, won a Nobel Prize in 2013.
Richard Norton Smith
Combining rigorous scholarship with gripping narrative, A.N. Wilson's "Victoria" gives us the finest biography
of the monarch since Elizabeth Longford's 1964 masterwork. With her Hanoverian temper and transparent
disregard for her heir, the amiable dullard Bertie, this tormented widow "screaming inside the royal straight
jacket" is decidedly more vulnerable, and less "Victorian," than the bonneted figurehead of legend. Her
charisma remains intact. So does her claim to greatness. In "The War That Ended Peace," Margaret
MacMillan has produced a sobering litany of official miscalculation disguised as the pursuit of national
interests. Among the gusher of books loosed by the centenary of World War I, this fascinating exploration of
Europe on the brink of Armageddon seems the best bet to take rank with Barbara Tuchman's 1962 classic,
"The Guns of August." As relevant as it is readable, Lynne Cheney's "James Madison: A Life Reconsidered"
achieves the near-impossible -- humanizing the most cerebral of America's Founders, our original
constitutional scholar, and a wartime president whose libertarian policies -- no one went to jail for criticizing
the liberty-loving Madison -- make him, arguably, the Un-Lincoln. Long overdue, and worth the wait.
-- Mr. Smith's most recent book is On His Own Terms: A Life of Nelson Rockefeller."
Rick Snyder
When I was younger, I used to read a great deal of nonfiction because I wanted to learn as much as I could.
Then, as I got older, I started reading more fiction as a sort of escape. But then I got into politics -- and
realized that what happens in real life is much crazier than fiction. One new novel I liked was by Mark
Russinovich, a Microsoft technical fellow: "Rogue Code" is a thrilling crime story about cybersecurity -- a topic
I'm very interested in. As for nonfiction, I recently enjoyed David McCullough's "The Path Between the Seas"
about the Panama Canal and Theodore Roosevelt after reading Doris Kearns Goodwin's "The Bully Pulpit." I
also liked Daniel James Brown's "The Boys in the Boat," about the American crew team and the 1936
Olympics.
-- Mr. Snyder is governor of Michigan.
Scott Stossel
One of the best new novels I read this year actually came out 16 years ago in England but was just published
in the United States: "On the Edge," Edward St. Aubyn's wry and biting yet at times moving send-up of New
Age culture, in which an existentially adrift middle-aged British banker pursues a woman to the wackier
reaches of California's meditation centers and spiritual-seeker communities. "On the Edge" is not as fiercely
witty and gemlike (or as dark) as his quintet of Patrick Melrose novels, but it is better than the other (quite
funny) novel he published this year, "Lost for Words." I would read anything Mr. St. Aubyn writes, including
his grocery lists and suicide notes. Other fiction: Marilynne Robinson's "Lila," the latest installment of her
Gilead series, which is about as far in style and sensibility from Mr. St. Aubyn as one can get. You read the
former to be filled with aesthetic bliss at the sharpness of the sentences and the darkness of the vision; you
read the latter to be filled with grace and hope. In "Let Me Be Frank With You," Richard Ford brings back
garrulous, observant Frank Bascombe, perhaps America's greatest postwar fictional middle-class white
everyman not named Rabbit Angstrom. And if you didn't think a Park Avenue dental practice could be the site
of an entertaining theological excavation of the human predicament, then you haven't read Joshua Ferris's
"To Rise Again at a Decent Hour." Nonfiction? "Little Failure," Gary Shteyngart's hilarious memoir. "The Trip
to Echo Spring," Olivia Laing's travelogue-cum-group-biography of the alcoholic misadventures of John
Cheever, Tennessee Williams, John Berryman, Ernest Hemingway, F. Scott Fitzgerald and Raymond Carver.
Speaking of Fitzgerald, "So We Read On: How the Great Gatsby Came to Be and Why It Endures," by
Maureen Corrigan, is an illuminating history and a close reading, through the lens of class, of what may be
the Great American Novel.

-- Mr. Stossel is editor of the Atlantic and the author of "My Age of Anxiety."
Diane von Furstenberg
Now that my own book is out, I have plenty of time to read, and I am so enjoying it. I recently finished
"Mademoiselle: Coco Chanel and the Pulse of History" by Rhonda Garelick, as well as the biography of Elsa
Schiaparelli by Meryle Secrest. Those two amazing designers, who were super rivals, were equally talented
and truly modern women. Both had shady periods, but their courage and their strength are very inspiring. I
also read "Year Zero" by Ian Buruma about 1945. It is quite horrifying to see in what state the world was
when World War II ended. With great joy, I read the latest Aharon Appelfeld novel, "Suddenly, Love," and as
usual his writing delighted me. Scott Anderson's "Lawrence in Arabia," a book that should enlighten me on
the state of the Middle East, is next on my list, as well as the new memoir by my friend Anjelica Huston.
-- Ms. von Furstenberg is a fashion designer and the author of "The Woman I Wanted to Be."
Adelle Waldman
This year I read two terrifically smart memoirs. In "There Was and There Was Not," Armenian-American
journalist Meline Toumani writes about trying to reconcile Western-style liberalism with the Armenian
community's animus toward Turkey because the country continues to deny the genocide. (In Turkey, it is
effectively illegal to call what happened a genocide.) As an adult, Ms. Toumani moved to Turkey and learned
the language. She hoped to achieve some sort of reconciliation; what she actually experienced is far more
complicated and raises many questions about nationalism and liberalism, politics and art. Fresh, lucid and
psychologically incisive, the book is also absorbing and beautifully written. Acclaimed biographer Blake Bailey
published a deft, affecting memoir about his troubled older brother, whose life was circumscribed by a
combination of mental illness, drug abuse and a profound, often tragic inability to get along with others. Deftly
written and unflinchingly honest, "The Splendid Things We Planned" recounts the brothers' childhood in
Oklahoma as sons of a successful attorney and a feisty, bookish, German-born mother and the ways the
boys' lives ultimately diverge. A gripping read, it's also a profoundly moving story of a family's attempts to help
his brother without being pulled down by him.
-- Ms. Waldman is the author of "The Love Affairs of Nathaniel P."
Scott Walker
Like me, Rep. Paul Ryan came of age during President Ronald Reagan's administration. His book, "The Way
Forward," applies a similar can-do optimism to the details of a blueprint to move our nation forward. Few, if
any, understand the complexities of our federal government's fiscal challenges better than Mr. Ryan. The fact
that he believes it is not too late to solve our problems should give us hope. President Reagan is an
inspiration to me, and, not surprisingly, I enjoyed "Reagan at Reykjavik." The author, Ken Adelman, served as
President Reagan's arms-control director. Because he was one of the few people with the president at the
1986 superpower summit in Iceland, his writing gave me a sense of what it actually was like to be there as
they negotiated with Mikhail Gorbachev and his delegation. Even though it is written by an admiring former
aide, "Reagan at Reykjavik" paints an interesting picture as it notes some of the president's weaknesses.
Ultimately, this book makes the case for Reagan's dogged determination and steady leadership -- not just at
Reykjavik but throughout his second term. Reading Mr. Adelman's book reminded me of President Reagan's
noble efforts to leave a better country and a better world for future generations.
-- Mr. Walker is governor of Wisconsin.
Alice Waters
I love everything about the way Maira Kalman sees the world, and so I've been relishing her book "My
Favorite Things." Each page has her signature evocative illustrations of the objects that matter most to her --
from a first edition "Winnie-the-Pooh" to Abraham Lincoln's pocket watch to her father's gray suit -- along with
profound and deeply moving observations on life. This year there were also remarkable first cookbooks from
two of the most talented chefs I know. Jean-Pierre Moulle, the longtime chef of Chez Panisse, collaborated
with his wife, Denise Moulle, to create "French Roots," a love song to the two countries that have shaped
their cooking. It is a collection of the sort of rustic, elegant dishes I always want to eat and beautiful,
ruminative essays on their lives in the United States and France. Just as wonderful is "Twelve Recipes," from
current Chez Panisse chef Cal Peternell. He wrote this originally for his son, who was leaving for college, and
its simple, delicious recipes resonate with young people who are learning to cook -- a real celebration of
home cooking.
-- Ms. Waters, a chef, is the owner of Chez Panisse in Berkeley, Calif.
Meg Wolitzer

--- Page 158 ---
Page 158 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Someone (I'm never sure who) once said, "Write as if everyone you know were dead," and Meghan Daum
really does write that way, by which I mean she writes what she wants to, without looking over her shoulder
every second. In "The Unspeakable" her eyes are fixed firmly on the page, as are the reader's. Her mother's
death, her own near-death experience, dogs, food, motherhood and, of course, Joni Mitchell -- all are
contained in a smart, strong and highly readable volume by this winning social critic. "Stone Mattress" by
Margaret Atwood offers wildly fun scenarios and pleasurably prickly moments in which characters reckon with
themselves and their pasts. You can tell she really had a good time writing and assembling this book, which
looks hard at old age, at rage and, more than once, at murderous impulses that live inside people.
-- Ms. Wolitzer's most recent book is "Belzhar."
Jacqueline Woodson
In "When I Was the Greatest," set in the Brooklyn of old, when brownstones were still chopped into
apartments and families could still find an affordable rental on a single salary, Jason Reynolds tells the story
of three young boys coming of age against a backdrop of absentee parents and Tourette syndrome. Brilliantly
told, this novel is heartbreaking, hysterical and thought-provoking. Mr. Reynolds is truly a writer to watch out
for. "Belzhar" by Meg Wolitzer made me run and find everything this writer has published. From page one of
this book, I was in and ready for the journey. This book will resonate with anyone who has ever been a
teenager. Mind blown!
-- Ms. Woodson is the author, most recently, of "Brown Girl Dreaming."

save $3 million
By Candace Jackson
1,531 words
12 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English

The Plaza Hotel's most prized apartments offer sweeping views of New York's Central Park. Suze Orman
wasn't sold.
Instead, in 2007 Ms. Orman and her wife, Kathy Travis, bought a 1,275-square-foot apartment in the Plaza
that faces the courtyard for $3.6 million. "The same apartment on the other side with park views was $3
million more," Ms. Orman explained. She also noted that by skipping the view, she and her wife can put
guests in a hotel room across the way that faces their living room. "We'll hold up a sign in the window that
says, 'breakfast is ready!'"
Ms. Orman, the 63-year-old personal finance expert and television personality, has made a name for herself
giving Americans straight-talking, tough-love advice on saving money, investing cautiously and not
overspending. Her oft-repeated mantra: "People first, then money, then things."
She takes a similarly pragmatic approach to her portfolio of properties, which includes a South Florida condo,
a waterfront parcel in the Bahamas and a condo near Johannesburg, South Africa. Ms. Orman said she
doesn't try to invest in real estate, saying it isn't "my forte." Instead, she buys places she likes -- often
relatively modest homes in nice locations -- and is willing to pay market rate for them. Despite current low
interest rates, for her own homes she likes to pay cash. "If I can't write a check for it, I can't afford it," she
said.
She can afford a fair amount. With 10 best-selling books and a long-running CNBC television show, Ms.
Orman declined to say what her personal net worth is today, but said it is well over the $25 million in liquid
assets that was reported by the New York Times several years ago.
When they bought their apartment in the Plaza Hotel -- the building had just converted to condominiums --
Ms. Orman said they initially found the grand-dame French Renaissance-style building, with its gold accents
and white-gloved butlers, "too fancy." They thought the apartment, in contrast, was too basic. Its heat and
air-conditioning were delivered via metal wall units and it had cheap aluminum fixtures and finishes. "It was
like a Motel 6!" said Ms. Orman.
Still, the Plaza had sentimental value -- Ms. Travis, who grew up in New Jersey, had stayed there with her
family -- and both liked the building's location and amenities, including room service, housekeeping and a
high-end food court, which opened since they moved in. "It could be like a nursing home," Ms. Orman joked.
"But really, when you're 63 you start to think like that!"
They spent almost a year renovating the space, and moved in shortly thereafter. Today, the one-bedroom
feels like a plush pied-a-terre with thick white crown moldings and herringbone hardwood floors, and Venetian
plaster and marble in the hall bathroom. There is a desk just off the living room, where Ms. Travis often works
while Ms. Orman works on her laptop in the bedroom. A Murphy bed folds down from the wall of the living
room.
Ms. Orman said she lowered the ceilings in the master suite and closet to install central heat and
air-conditioning but left the living room ceilings high. "When you have a small unit, what gives it grandeur are
the high ceilings." Small closets have been added to hold everything from the couple's china to
leather-bound, first-edition copies of Ms. Orman's books. She's even stored her two Emmy Awards. "Suze
can't live with clutter," said Ms. Travis, 62, who is also Ms. Orman's manager.
Looking toward her gray couch and silvery silk wall covering, Ms. Orman, who has short blond side-swept
hair and a Chicago accent, threw up her hands and said, in her typically exclamatory way, the Emmys are
"gold -- so they don't go!"

In 2004, Ms. Orman and Ms. Travis bought their primary home, a 2,175-square-foot condo with big windows
and 600-square-feet of outdoor space, in Hillsboro Beach, Fla., for $954,000. Initially, the couple came to the
area to be closer to both of their mothers, who have since died. They ended up "absolutely falling madly in
love with Florida."
Though real-estate agents tried to steer them toward a larger penthouse, Ms. Orman said she and Ms. Travis
decided to purchase a smaller place on the second floor, which felt closer to the water and was plenty large
enough for the two of them. Ms. Orman said it is in a "totally regular" condo complex in between the beach
and the Intracoastal Waterway, with a swimming pool but few other amenities.
Earlier this year, while she and Ms. Travis were on a fishing trip in the Bahamas, they came across a
waterfront parcel near where they were docked. Ms. Orman said it was up for sale because the previous
owner lost her money investing with Bernie Madoff. "On the spot I said, 'I'll take it'!" she recalled. She said
they paid $2.5 million for the property, which turned out to be roughly equal to the proceeds from the sale of a
53-foot Azimut yacht and a San Francisco home. "I'm not, at this point in my career, trying to increase
expenses," said Ms. Orman.
Now they are building a small waterfront home with 14-foot-ceilings that Ms. Orman said will be completed in
about a year. "I told [the previous owner] when it's done she's always welcome to come back and stay with
us," said Ms. Orman, who said they are considering retiring there.
Ms. Orman grew up on the South Side of Chicago, and began working at her father's deli when she was 11
years old. Her personal story, which is recounted in her books, has become a part of her brand. After college,
she worked as a waitress for seven years at a Berkeley cafe. One of her regular customers lent her the
money to start her own restaurant, with contributions from other regulars. On the customer's advice, Ms.
Orman put the money into a money-market account at Merrill Lynch until she was ready to launch.
A Merrill Lynch broker persuaded her to put her savings into a high-risk investment account, Ms. Orman said,
and within three months, her savings were wiped out. Dismayed, she began studying the financial markets
and decided to apply for a job herself at Merrill Lynch to become a broker. Meanwhile, she sued the company
over her loss.
Ms. Orman got the job as a broker at Merrill, and learned that her lawsuit allowed her to work with little fear of
being fired (companies can be hesitant to fire employees in the midst of litigation). Eventually, the company
settled with her for the entire amount invested plus interest, which Ms. Orman said she subsequently returned
to her customers.
She opened her own firm in 1987. In 1995 she wrote her first book, "You've Earned It, Don't Lose It," and has
since wrote nine more, with titles like "The Courage to be Rich" and "The Money Book for the Young,
Fabulous and Broke." Ms. Orman's been a regular on Oprah Winfrey's show and network, and in 2002 her
show made its debut on CNBC.
She doesn't collect properties she doesn't use regularly. In March, the couple sold their 2,000-square-foot
Edwardian home in San Francisco. "It was our best house!" said Ms. Travis. But they were only able to use it
about two weeks a year with Ms. Orman's schedule.
Ms. Orman said she would soon let go of her condo in Northcliff, a suburb of Johannesburg. She purchased it
after spending time in the country in 2000 and falling in love with the area. A business partner built a condo
development and, with the favorable exchange rate, Ms. Orman thought it was a good place to have a
foothold for investments. But she hasn't visited the country since she and Ms. Travis married there in 2010.
She spent about $400,000 on the apartment, which is about 2,500-square-feet with "stunning views." The
home is currently on the market for about the amount she paid for it, said Ms. Orman.
"I'm not sad to let real estate go. Ever," said Ms. Orman. "My real estate and the things I own don't define me.
I could care less if I lived in a tent somewhere."
Ms. Travis said Ms. Orman's aversion to holding on to extra stuff means they are constantly getting rid of old
clothes and organizing. On a recent morning, Ms. Orman pointed out a few of her signature colorful jackets
that were ready to be donated. "How many damn jackets does one need?" said Ms. Orman.

Demand at 30-Year Sale Of Treasurys Is Strong
Credit Markets
Demand at 30-Year Sale Of Treasurys Is Strong
By Min Zeng
743 words
12 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The price of the 30-year U.S. Treasury bond strengthened after a $13 billion sale of that maturity drew the
highest demand in nearly two years.
The buying sent the yield on the 30-year bond down to 2.827%, the lowest closing level since December
2012. The yield was 2.835% on Wednesday. Yields fall as prices rise.
The auction result underscores the broad appeal of long-dated Treasury bonds, whose returns this year have
beaten those for stocks and gold.
Prices of benchmark 10-year Treasury notes pulled back Thursday for the first time this week, as an upbeat
U.S. consumer-spending report sapped demand for haven assets.
The 10-year yield rose to 2.178%, from 2.169% a day earlier. The yield remains near the lowest level of the
year and has fallen from 3% at the start of January.
Demand for long-dated Treasury bonds has been fired up this year by uneven global economic growth and
low inflation, which have boosted the allure of highly liquid financial assets even as U.S. economic growth
has gained momentum.
Treasury bonds maturing in more than 25 years have posted a total return of 25.7% this year through
Wednesday, according to data from Barclays. The overall Treasury debt market has returned 4.97%.
The S&P 500 stock index has posted a total return of 11.76% over the same period. Meanwhile, gold was up
2.2%, while U.S. crude oil plunged 38%. Total return for stocks and bonds includes price changes and
interest or dividend payments.
The 30-year Treasury bond has drawn buyers because it offers the highest yield in the U.S. government-bond
market. The yield also is higher than those of comparable-maturity government bonds from Germany, the
U.K., Japan and France.
"Global yields are so low that there is overwhelming demand for high-quality higher-yielding assets, and the
long end of the U.S. bond market looks like a high yielder to Asian and European money mangers and
reserve managers," said Tom Tucci, head of Treasury trading in New York at CIBC World Markets Corp.
Buyers piled into Thursday's 30-year bond sale even as they ended up getting a yield of 2.848%, the lowest
for the maturity since November 2012.
Indirect bidding at the auction, a gauge of foreign demand, increased to 49.8%, the highest since July. A $21
billion sale of 10-year Treasury notes on Wednesday drew the strongest foreign demand since December
2011.
Continued selloffs in crude oil since the summer have pushed down inflation worries, another factor that
boosted buying of the 30-year bonds.
Inflation chips away investors' fixed returns over time and is the main threat to the value of Treasury debt
maturing in 10 to 30 years.
"When you buy a bond, you are buying a fixed cash flow. So lower inflation enhances it," said Jim Caron,
global fixed-income portfolio manager at Morgan Stanley Investment Management, which had $396 billion of
assets under management at June 30.
U.S. crude oil fell 1.6% Thursday and settled at $59.95 a barrel, the lowest since July 2009.

--- Page 162 ---
Page 162 of 215 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The 30-year bond's price had fallen earlier Thursday, after U.S. retail sales climbed 0.7% in November from a
month earlier, the biggest jump in eight months.
The release was the latest evidence that the world's largest economy has gained traction, bolstering
investors' expectations that stronger growth will allow the Federal Reserve to start raising interest rates next
year for the first time since 2006.
"Given the amount of buying that has already taken place out there, it is starting to crack bond prices a bit as
the economic data is starting to register," said Thomas Roth, executive director in the U.S. government-bond
trading group at Mitsubishi UFJ Securities (USA) Inc. in New York.
A strengthening economy and the prospect of tightening monetary policy would typically reduce the appeal
of holding ultrasafe U.S. government bonds. But benchmark Treasury yields have fallen this year as weaker
growth overseas and falling energy prices have raised concerns over global economic growth. Some
investors are concerned about whether faltering growth overseas may drag down U.S. growth momentum. In
this case, the Fed may be slow in shifting into higher interest rates, they said.
